does not consist of other sentences and does not contain quantifiers. Examples include \"Plato snores,\" \"Bill is taller than Mary,\" and \"Bill is standing between Mary and John.\"\n\n2. **Molecular Sentence:** A molecular sentence is defined as a sentence that either consists of other sentences or contains a quantifier. Examples include \"Bill is tall and Sally is smart\" and \"someone snores.\"\n\n3. **Open Sentence:** An open sentence is defined as a sentence with a variable that can be replaced with an expression to form a true or false statement. Examples include ‹x snores›, ‹x is taller than y›, and ‹x is standing between y and z›.\n\n4. **Function:** A function is defined as a rule that assigns truth-values to objects or sets of objects based on their properties. For example, the open sentence ‹x snores› is seen as a function that assigns truth to each snorer and falsehood to each thing that doesn't snore.\n\n5. **Truth-Value:** A truth-value is defined as either true or false, which can be assigned to a sentence based on the properties of its arguments.\n\n### 7.2 Technical Vocabulary Introduced or Employed\n\n1. **Ordered Pair:** An ordered pair is a set of two objects in which the order of the objects matters. For example, the ordered pair <Bill, Mary> is used to represent the sentence \"Bill is taller than Mary.\"\n\n2. **Ordered Triple:** An ordered triple is a set of three objects in which the order of the objects matters. For example, the ordered triple <Bill, Mary, John> is used to represent the sentence \"Bill is standing between Mary and John.\"\n\n### 7.3 Distinctions Drawn Between Related Concepts\n\n1. **Atomic truth-values of sentences.\n\n### 8.6 Dialectical Structure\n\n1. **Thesis:** Frege's approach to language treats all atomic sentences as having a unified form, where each sentence can be represented as a function that assigns truth-values to objects or ordered sets of objects.\n\n2. **Antithesis:** Pre-Fregean logicians treated different types of atomic sentences as having different forms based on their grammatical surface structure, which made it impossible for them to formalize inferences involving atomic sentences effectively.\n\n3. **Synthesis:** Frege's approach, by looking beyond the grammatical surface structure to the underlying logical structure of sentences, overcomes the limitations of the pre-Fregean approach and allows for a more unified and effective formalization of atomic sentences.\n\n## SECTION 9: CRITICAL EVALUATION INDICATORS\n\n### 9.1 Strengths of the Argument\n\n1. **Clarity and Precision:** The text's argument is clear and precise, using formal notation and examples to illustrate Frege's approach to treating sentences as functions that assign truth-values.\n\n2. **Systematic Approach:** The text presents a systematic approach to analyzing the logical structure of sentences, showing how all atomic sentences can be represented using a unified form.\n\n3. **Overcoming Limitations:** The text effectively shows how Frege's approach overcomes the limitations of pre-Fregean logic, which was hindered by the grammatical surface structure of sentences.\n\n### 9.2 Potential Weaknesses or Gaps\n\n1. **Limited Scope:** The text focuses on atomic sentences and does not address how Frege's approach can be extended to molecular sentences, which may limit its applicability to more complex forms of language.\n\n2. **Lack of Empirical Evidence:** The text does not provide empirical evidence to support its claims, relying instead on logical analysis and examples, which may weaken its persuasiveness for some readers.\n\n3. **Neglect of Nuances:** The text's focus on the logical structure of sentences may neglect the nuances and complexities of natural language, which could be seen as a weakness by those who emphasize the importance of context and pragmatics in understanding meaning.\n\n### 9.3 Unstated Assumptions that Could be Challenged\n\n1. **Assumption of Objectivity:** The text assumes that truth-values are objective and can be assigned based on the properties of objects or sets of objects, which could be challenged by those who argue that truth-values are subjective or context-dependent.\n\n2. **Assumption of Universality:** The text assumes that Frege's approach is universally applicable to all atomic sentences, which could be challenged by those who argue that different types of sentences may require different approaches to analysis.\n\n3. **Assumption of Logical Structure:** The text assumes that the logical structure of sentences is more fundamental than their grammatical form, which could be challenged by those who argue that the grammatical form of sentences is essential for understanding their meaning.\n\n### 9.4 Alternative Interpretations or Objections Not Addressed\n\n1. **Alternative Interpretation:** Some might argue that Frege's approach is not the only way to formalize atomic sentences, and that other approaches, such as those based on model theory or proof theory, could be equally effective.\n\n2. **Objection Not Addressed:** The text does not address the objection that Frege's approach may not be able to account for the nuances and complexities of natural language, such as ambiguity, context-sensitivity, and pragmatic factors.\n\n### 9.5 Scope and Limitations of the Claims\n\n1. **Scope:** The text's claims are limited to atomic sentences and the nuances and complexities of natural language, which could be seen as a limitation by those who emphasize the importance of context and pragmatics in understanding meaning.\n\n## SECTION 10: ## SECTION 1: DOMAIN & DISCIPLINARY CONTEXT

### 1.1 Primary Academic Domain(s) and Subdomain(s)
1. **Philosophy of Language**: The text falls primarily within the philosophy of language, a subdomain of philosophy that focuses on the nature, origin, and use of language.
2. **Linguistics**: Specifically, the text engages with theoretical linguistics, which deals with the abstract structure of language and its components.

### 1.2 Interdisciplinary Connections and Influences
1. **Cognitive Science**: The discussion on the psychological aspects of meaning connects to cognitive science, which studies mental processes and their role in language.
2. **Psychology**: The text's exploration of psychological meaning ties into psychology, particularly in the areas of intentionality and communication.
3. **Logic**: The analysis of meaning and its implications for truth and falsity relates to formal logic, which deals with the principles of correct reasoning.

### 1.3 Historical Positioning Within the Field
1. **Early 20th Century**: The text's engagement with the concept of meaning can be traced back to early 20th-century philosophers like Frege, who distinguished between sense and reference, and Wittgenstein, who explored the nature of language and meaning.
2. **Mid-20th Century**: The reference to Chomsky's work on meaningless sentences situates the text within the mid-20th-century debates on generative grammar and the nature of linguistic competence.

### 1.4 Relationship to Major Schools of Thought
1. **Analytic Philosophy**: The text's focus on precise definitions and logical analysis aligns with the analytic tradition, which emphasizes clarity and rigor in philosophical inquiry.
2. **Pragmatism**: The discussion of psychological meaning and its relation to intentionality resonates with pragmatic theories of meaning, which emphasize the practical use of language.

### 1.5 Target Audience and Scholarly Community
1. **Philosophers of Language**: The primary audience includes philosophers specializing in language, who would be interested in the nuanced analysis of meaning.
2. **Linguists**: Theoretical linguists would find the text relevant, particularly those interested in the semantics and pragmatics of language.
3. **Cognitive Scientists**: Researchers in cognitive science, especially those studying language processing and communication, would benefit from the text's insights into psychological meaning.

## SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

### 2.1 Core Thesis Statement
1. **Primary Formulation**: The concept of language is fundamentally tied to the concept of meaning, and understanding the nature of language requires distinguishing between different types of meaning.
2. **Secondary Formulation**: Linguistic expressions are inherently meaningful, and the concept of meaning can be categorized into evidential, psychological, and linguistic meanings, with linguistic meaning being distinct and foundational.

### 2.2 Primary Arguments Supporting the Thesis
1. **Argument 1: The Necessity of Meaning for Expressions**
   - **Explanation**: The text asserts that expressions, which are essential components of language, must be meaningful. This is based on the premise that nothing meaningless can be considered an expression, thereby linking the concept of language directly to meaning.
2. **Argument 2: The Three Meanings of 'Meaning'**
   - **Explanation**: The text identifies three distinct meanings of the word 'meaning': evidential, psychological, and linguistic. Each type is analyzed in detail, with the linguistic meaning being highlighted as the most relevant to understanding language.
3. **Argument 3: The Distinction Between Psychological and Linguistic Meaning**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that psychological meaning (what speakers intend to convey) is distinct from linguistic meaning (the inherent meaning of sentences). This distinction is crucial for understanding how language functions and how meaning is conveyed.

### 2.3 Secondary Arguments and Subsidiary Claims
1. **Subsidiary Claim 1: The Rejection of Meaningless Expressions**
   - **Explanation**: The text challenges the notion of meaningless expressions, using Chomsky's example to argue that what might seem meaningless can be interpreted as either false or vacuously true, thus retaining meaning.
2. **Subsidiary Claim 2: The Parasitic Nature of Psychological Meaning**
   - **Explanation**: The text claims that psychological meaning is parasitic on linguistic meaning, suggesting that speakers' intentions rely on the pre-existing meanings of sentences.

### 2.4 Implicit Assumptions Underlying the Arguments
1. **Assumption 1: The Primacy of Meaning in Language**
   - **Explanation**: The text assumes that meaning is central to the concept of language, which underpins the entire analysis.
2. **Assumption 2: The Possibility of Clear Distinctions**
   - **Explanation**: The text assumes that it is possible to clearly distinguish between different types of meaning, which is necessary for the argument's structure.

### 2.5 Logical Structure of the Overall Argument
1. **Premise 1**: Expressions are essential to language.
2. **Premise 2**: Expressions must be meaningful.
3. **Premise 3**: The word 'meaning' has three distinct meanings: evidential, psychological, and linguistic.
4. **Premise 4**: Linguistic meaning is distinct from and foundational to psychological meaning.
5. **Conclusion**: Understanding language requires understanding the nature of linguistic meaning and its distinction from other types of meaning.

### 2.6 Argument Progression and Development Throughout the Text
1. **Introduction of the Concept of Meaning**: The text begins by establishing the necessity of meaning for expressions and language.
2. **Analysis of Different Meanings**: It then proceeds to analyze the three types of meaning, starting with evidential meaning, moving to psychological meaning, and finally focusing on linguistic meaning.
3. **Distinction Between Psychological and Linguistic Meaning**: The text elaborates on the distinction between psychological and linguistic meaning, using examples to illustrate the parasitic nature of psychological meaning.
4. **Rejection of Meaningless Expressions**: The text challenges the notion of meaningless expressions, using Chomsky's example to argue that all expressions have some form of meaning.
5. **Conclusion**: The text concludes by reinforcing the centrality of linguistic meaning to the concept of language.

## SECTION 3: REPRESENTATIVE QUOTATIONS (Minimum 10 quotations)

1. **Quote 1**: "There would be no languages if there were no expressions (words, phrases, sentences, etc.). Nothing meaningless is an expression."
   - **Location/Context**: Section 1.0, opening paragraph.
   - **Significance**: This quote establishes the foundational premise that expressions, and thus language, must be meaningful.

2. **Quote 2**: "But it isn't much use to be told that words and sentences 'have meanings,' since the word 'meaning' has three different meanings, and only one of these directly relates to the nature of language."
   - **Location/Context**: Section 1.0, second paragraph.
   - **Significance**: This quote introduces the complexity of the concept of meaning and sets the stage for the detailed analysis that follows.

3. **Quote 3**: "Chomsky famously said that 'colorless green dreams sleep furiously' is both meaningless and is a bona fide sentence, and therefore expression, of English. This reasoning is no good."
   - **Location/Context**: Section 1.0, third paragraph.
   - **Significance**: This quote challenges a well-known example of a supposedly meaningless sentence, arguing that it can be interpreted as having meaning.

4. **Quote 4**: "Meaning #1: The evidential meaning of 'meaning' In some cases, to say that x 'means' y is to say that x is evidence of y-that x and y are causally interrelated in such a way that, given x, it can reasonably be inferred that y."
   - **Location/Context**: Section 1.0, under Meaning #1.
   - **Significance**: This quote defines the first type of meaning, evidential meaning, and explains its causal nature.

5. **Quote 5**: "Meaning #2: The psychological meaning of 'meaning' When we use sentences, we mean things by them. Meaning in this sense is a psychological notion."
   - **Location/Context**: Section 1.0, under Meaning #2.
   - **Significance**: This quote introduces the second type of meaning, psychological meaning, and its connection to intentions.

6. **Quote 6**: "Meaning #3: The linguistic meaning of 'meaning' The sentence 'snow is white' says something about something; it attributes the property of being white to snow. Therefore, it means that snow is white."
   - **Location/Context**: Section 1.0, under Meaning #3.
   - **Significance**: This quote defines the third type of meaning, linguistic meaning, and its role in attributing properties.

7. **Quote 7**: "Meaning in the psychological sense involves, but does not coincide with, meaning in the linguistic sense."
   - **Location/Context**: Section 1.0, under Meaning #3.
   - **Significance**: This quote highlights the distinction between psychological and linguistic meaning, emphasizing their interrelation.

8. **Quote 8**: "My meaning that Sally is devious is parasitic on my utterance's meaning that things aren't always as they seem."
   - **Location/Context**: Section 1.0, under Meaning #3.
   - **Significance**: This quote illustrates the parasitic nature of psychological meaning on linguistic meaning, using an example.

9. **Quote 9**: "Some philosophers and linguists have held that for: (1) 'snow is white' to mean that snow is white is for it to be the case that, in uttering 'snow is white,' what people mean is that snow is white."
   - **Location/Context**: Section 1.2, opening paragraph.
   - **Significance**: This quote introduces a view that the text aims to refute, setting up the argument for the distinction between linguistic and psychological meaning.

10. **Quote 10**: "The sentence: (2) 'the cube root of three is Sir Lawrence Olivier's favorite irrational number between one and four' has a determinate meaning, even though that sentence has never been uttered before and, therefore, no one as of yet has ever meant anything by it."
    - **Location/Context**: Section 1.2, second paragraph.
    - **Significance**: This quote provides an example to support the argument that sentences can have determinate meanings independent of their being uttered or intended.

## SECTION 4: ANALYTICAL MOVES DEPLOYED

### 4.1 Conceptual Analysis and Distinctions Drawn

   - **Where it Appears**: Section 1.0, third paragraph, with the example of "colorless green dreams sleep furiously."
   - **How it Functions**: By showing that what might seem meaningless can be interpreted as either false or vacuously true, the text argues against the possibility of truly meaningless expressions.

2. **Modus Tollens**: The text employs this strategy to refute the view that linguistic meaning is reducible to psychological meaning.
   - **Where it Appears**: Section 1.2, where the text argues that sentences can have determinate meanings even if they have never been uttered.
   - **How it Functions**: By showing that sentences can have meanings independent of speakers' intentions, the text refutes the claim that linguistic meaning is solely dependent on psychological meaning.

## SECTION 7: CONCEPTUAL APPARATUS

### 7.1 Key Terms and Their Definitions
1. **Expression**: Defined as a meaningful unit of language, such as a word, phrase, or sentence.
2. **Meaning**: Analyzed into three types: evidential, psychological, and linguistic.
   - **Evidential Meaning**: When x means y, x is evidence of y, and x and y are causally interrelated.
   - **Psychological Meaning**: The meaning intended by a speaker when using a sentence.
   - **Linguistic Meaning**: The inherent meaning of a sentence, independent of speakers' intentions.

### 7.2 Technical Vocabulary Introduced or Employed
1. **Parasitic**: Used to describe the relationship between psychological and linguistic meaning, where psychological meaning depends on linguistic meaning.
2. **Vacuously True**: A statement that is true because it asserts something about a non-existent or empty set.

### 7.3 Distinctions Drawn Between Related Concepts
1. **Evidential vs. Psychological Meaning**: Evidential meaning involves causal relationships and evidence, while psychological meaning involves speakers' intentions.
2. **Psychological vs. Linguistic Meaning**: Psychological meaning is what speakers intend to convey, while linguistic meaning is the inherent meaning of sentences.

### 7.4 Theoretical Innovations or Neologisms
1. **No Neologisms**: The text does not introduce new terms but rather clarifies and distinguishes existing concepts.

### 7.5 Repurposing of Existing Concepts
1. **Meaning**: The text repurposes the concept of meaning by breaking it down into three distinct types and analyzing their interrelations.

### 8.1 Overall Organization of the Argument
1. **Introduction**: The text begins by establishing the necessity of meaning for expressions and language.

4. **Rejection of Meaningless Expressions**: The text challenges the notion of meaningless expressions, using Chomsky's example to argue that all expressions have some form of meaning.
5. **Conclusion**: The text concludes by reinforcing the centrality of linguistic meaning to the concept of language.

### 8.2 Logical Dependencies Between Claims

2. **Dependency 2**: The distinction between psychological and linguistic meaning depends on the analysis of the three types of meaning.
3. **Dependency 3**: The rejection of meaningless expressions depends on the argument that all expressions can be interpreted as having some form of meaning.

### 8.3 Progression from Premises to Conclusions
1. **Premise to Conclusion 1**: From the premise that expressions must be meaningful, the text concludes that language is fundamentally tied to meaning.
2. **Premise to Conclusion 2**: From the analysis of the three types of meaning, the text concludes that linguistic meaning is the most relevant to understanding language.
3. **Premise to Conclusion 3**: From the distinction between psychological and linguistic meaning, the text concludes that linguistic meaning is foundational and independent of speakers' intentions.

### 8.4 Use of Examples, Illustrations, or Case Studies
1. **Example 1**: "Smith's hacking cough means that he has a violent lung infection" is used to illustrate evidential meaning.
2. **Example 2**: "Things aren't always as they seem" is used to illustrate psychological meaning and its parasitic nature on linguistic meaning.
3. **Example 3**: "Snow is white" is used to illustrate linguistic meaning.
4. **Example 4**: "Colorless green dreams sleep furiously" is used to challenge the notion of meaningless expressions.
5. **Example 5**: "The cube root of three is Sir Lawrence Olivier's favorite irrational number between one and four" is used to argue that sentences can have determinate meanings independent of their being uttered or intended.

### 8.5 Anticipation and Response to Objections
1. **Objection 1**: The view that linguistic meaning can be reduced to psychological meaning.
   - **Response**: The text refutes this view by arguing that sentences can have determinate meanings even if they have never been uttered or intended.
2. **Objection 2**: The notion of meaningless expressions.
   - **Response**: The text challenges this notion by arguing that all expressions can be interpreted as having some form of meaning.

### 8.6 Dialectical Structure
1. **Thesis**: The concept of language is fundamentally tied to the concept of meaning, and understanding the nature of language requires distinguishing between different types of meaning.
2. **Antithesis**: The view that linguistic meaning can be reduced to psychological meaning and the notion of meaningless expressions.
3. **Synthesis**: The text synthesizes its arguments by reinforcing the centrality of linguistic meaning to the concept of language and refuting opposing views.

## SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

### 2.1 Core Thesis Statement
1. **Primary Formulation**: The core thesis of the text is that linguistic meaning and psychological meaning are distinct, with psychological meaning being parasitic on linguistic meaning.
2. **Secondary Formulation**: The text also argues that the relationship between a sentence and its meaning is conventional and not purely causal, distinguishing it from natural signs like smoke and fire.

### 2.2 Primary Arguments Supporting the Thesis
1. **Argument 1: Distinction Between Linguistic and Psychological Meaning**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that the meaning of a sentence (linguistic meaning) is not identical to what a speaker means by uttering that sentence (psychological meaning). This is supported by the example of the sentence "snow is white," where the linguistic meaning is fixed by convention, while the psychological meaning can vary based on the speaker's intentions.
2. **Argument 2: Psychological Meaning Depends on Linguistic Meaning**
   - **Explanation**: The text posits that for a speaker to convey a psychological meaning, they must first understand the linguistic meaning of the sentence they are using. This is illustrated by the example where a speaker cannot sincerely and literally convey that snow is white if they believe the sentence "snow is white" means something else.
3. **Argument 3: Conventionality of Linguistic Meaning**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that linguistic meaning is a matter of convention, unlike natural signs like smoke and fire. This is supported by the example that the meaning of "snow is white" is determined by linguistic conventions, not by a direct causal relationship with the whiteness of snow.

### 2.3 Secondary Arguments and Subsidiary Claims
1. **Subsidiary Claim 1: Utterances Involve Judgments**
   - **Explanation**: The text claims that utterances of sentences like "snow is white" involve judgments about the world and about language, which adds a normative dimension to language use.
2. **Subsidiary Claim 2: No Meaningless Expressions**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues against the notion of meaningless expressions, suggesting that what are often considered meaningless are either non-expressions, absurd but meaningful sentences, or homonyms used in unconventional contexts.

### 2.4 Implicit Assumptions Underlying the Arguments
1. **Assumption 1: Language is Conventional**: The text assumes that language is fundamentally conventional, which is crucial for its argument about the distinction between linguistic and psychological meaning.
2. **Assumption 2: Speakers Have Intentions**: The text assumes that speakers have intentions when they use language, which is necessary for the concept of psychological meaning.
3. **Assumption 3: Context Matters**: The text assumes that the context in which a sentence is uttered plays a significant role in determining its meaning, which is implicit in its discussion of psychological meaning.

### 2.5 Logical Structure of the Overall Argument
1. **Premise 1**: Linguistic meaning is determined by convention.
2. **Premise 2**: Psychological meaning is what a speaker intends to convey.
3. **Premise 3**: Psychological meaning depends on the speaker's understanding of linguistic meaning.
4. **Conclusion**: Therefore, linguistic meaning and psychological meaning are distinct, with psychological meaning being parasitic on linguistic meaning.

### 2.6 Argument Progression and Development Throughout the Text
1. **Introduction of Distinction**: The text begins by introducing the distinction between linguistic and psychological meaning, using the example of "snow is white."
2. **Elaboration on Dependence**: It then elaborates on how psychological meaning depends on linguistic meaning, using further examples to illustrate this point.
3. **Conventionality of Linguistic Meaning**: The text shifts to discuss the conventionality of linguistic meaning, contrasting it with natural signs.
4. **Normative Dimension**: It introduces the normative dimension of language use, explaining how utterances involve judgments.
5. **Addressing Meaningless Expressions**: Finally, the text addresses the notion of meaningless expressions, arguing against their existence and providing reasons for common misconceptions.

   - **Location/Context**: This quote is part of the argument about the conventionality of linguistic meaning.

8. **Quote 8**: "Utterances of 'snow is white' embody judgments about the color of snow and about how, linguistic conventions being what they are, one can report the color of snow."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the discussion of the normative dimension of language use.

## SECTION 7: CONCEPTUAL APPARATUS

### 7.1 Key Terms and Their Definitions
1. **Linguistic Meaning**: The conventional meaning of a sentence, determined by linguistic rules and conventions.
2. **Psychological Meaning**: What a speaker intends to convey when uttering a sentence, dependent on their beliefs about the sentence's linguistic meaning.
3. **Convention**: A shared agreement among speakers of a language that determines the meaning of words and sentences.
4. **Proposition**: A statement or assertion that can be true or false, serving as the bearer of meaning.

### 7.2 Technical Vocabulary Introduced or Employed

## SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

### 2.1 Core Thesis Statement

The core thesis of the text can be articulated in multiple formulations due to its complexity:

1. **Primary Formulation**: The text argues that sentences are structurally isomorphic to propositions, which are digital structures composed of discrete parts, and that the study of language can be divided into three branches: semantics, syntax, and pragmatics.

2. **Secondary Formulation**: The text posits that the meaning of a sentence is a proposition, which is distinct from the sentence itself but structurally similar, and that language use involves both literal and non-literal meanings, which are studied by semantics and pragmatics, respectively.

### 2.2 Primary Arguments Supporting the Thesis

1. **Sentences as Proposition-Isomorphs**:
   - The text argues that sentences express propositions, which are not themselves sentences but are structurally similar to them. This is supported by the example of different sentences expressing the same proposition, such as "schnee ist weiss" and "snow is white."
   - The argument is further developed by discussing how sentences like "John loves Mary" and "Sally punched John" share the word "John," indicating that propositions have discrete parts that correspond to parts of sentences.

2. **Propositions as Digital Structures**:
   - The text asserts that propositions, like sentences, are digital structures composed of discrete, isolable entities. This is contrasted with visual perceptions, which are analog structures.
   - The argument is supported by the example of the sentence "Sally punched Bob," which can be decomposed into minimal units of significance (morphemes), suggesting that propositions have a similar structure.

3. **Three Branches of the Philosophy of Language**:
   - The text divides the study of language into semantics, syntax, and pragmatics, each focusing on different aspects of language: literal meaning, structure of complex expressions, and use of language, respectively.
   - Each branch is exemplified: semantics with the literal meaning of a boss's statement, pragmatics with the non-literal use of language, and syntax with the structure of complex expressions like "the man who ate my cookie."

### 2.3 Secondary Arguments and Subsidiary Claims

1. **The Absolute as a Sentence-Imposter**:
   - The text claims that "the absolute is one" is not a sentence but a sentence-imposter because the word "absolute" in this context is a mere homonym and lacks semantic assignment.
   - This argument is used to illustrate the importance of semantic rules in determining whether an expression is a linguistic expression.

2. **Propositions and Truths**:
   - The text asserts that propositions, when true, are truths and have existed as long as there have been truths, implying that propositions are not human creations.
   - This claim supports the idea that propositions are fundamental to understanding the nature of language and reality.

### 2.4 Implicit Assumptions Underlying the Arguments

1. **Semantic Realism**: The text assumes a form of semantic realism, where propositions exist independently of human language and are the bearers of truth values.

2. **Compositionality**: The argument that sentences are structurally similar to propositions assumes the principle of compositionality, where the meaning of a complex expression is determined by the meanings of its parts and their mode of combination.

3. **Distinction Between Literal and Non-Literal Meaning**: The text assumes a clear distinction between literal and non-literal meanings, which is crucial for the arguments in semantics and pragmatics.

### 2.5 Logical Structure of the Overall Argument

The text's overall argument is structured as follows:

1. **Introduction of Key Concepts**: The text begins by introducing the concept of sentences as proposition-isomorphs and the nature of propositions as digital structures.

2. **Division of the Philosophy of Language**: It then divides the study of language into three branches-semantics, syntax, and pragmatics-and explains each branch's focus and relevance.

3. **Detailed Analysis of Each Branch**: The text provides detailed examples and explanations for each branch, illustrating how they contribute to understanding language.

4. **Integration of Concepts**: Finally, it integrates these concepts to argue that sentences are structurally isomorphic to propositions, which are digital structures, and that the study of language must consider both literal and non-literal meanings.

### 2.6 Argument Progression and Development Throughout the Text

1. **Initial Claim**: The text starts with the claim that "the absolute is one" is not a sentence but a sentence-imposter, setting the stage for a discussion on the nature of linguistic expressions.

2. **Development of Proposition Concept**: It then develops the concept of propositions, arguing that they are distinct from sentences but structurally similar, and that they are digital structures.

3. **Introduction of Language Branches**: The text introduces the three branches of the philosophy of language, providing a framework for understanding different aspects of language.

4. **Detailed Analysis**: Each branch is analyzed in detail, with examples illustrating their relevance and application.

5. **Synthesis**: The text concludes by synthesizing these concepts, reinforcing the idea that sentences are proposition-isomorphs and that the study of language must consider both its structure and use.

2. **Quote**: "Sentences and propositions are digital structures, meaning that they have unique breakdowns into minimal significant units."
   - **Location/Context**: Section 2.1, Propositions as digital structures.

   - **Location/Context**: Section 2.0, Sentences as proposition-isomorphs.
   - **Significance**: This quote supports the argument that propositions are fundamental to reality and not merely human constructs, reinforcing the idea that they are the bearers of truth values.

   - **Location/Context**: Section 2.0, Sentences as proposition-isomorphs.

### 3.3 Crucial Definitions or Conceptual Distinctions

6. **Quote**: "A complex expression is one that consists of other expressions."
   - **Location/Context**: Section 3.3, Syntax.

### 3.4 Positions Being Criticized or Refuted

7. **Quote**: "What occurs in 'the absolute is one' is not the word 'absolute', but a mere homonym of that word."
   - **Location/Context**: Section 1, The absolute as a sentence-imposter.
   - **Significance**: This quote criticizes the notion that "the absolute is one" is a meaningful sentence, arguing that the word "absolute" in this context lacks semantic assignment and is thus not a linguistic expression.

### 3.5 Methodological Statements

8. **Quote**: "The discipline of semantics attempts to make it clear what our utterances literally mean."
   - **Location/Context**: Section 3.1, Semantics.
   - **Significance**: This quote outlines the methodological approach of semantics, which focuses on the literal meaning of utterances and excludes considerations of suggestion or innuendo.

9. **Quote**: "The discipline of pragmatics tries to identify the remaining factors [governing what a sentence is used to mean]."
   - **Location/Context**: Section 3.2, Pragmatics.
   - **Significance**: This quote describes the methodological approach of pragmatics, which studies the use of language and the factors beyond literal meaning that influence what a sentence is used to convey.

### 3.6 Pivotal Turns in the Argument

10. **Quote**: "Thus, what a sentence literally means is only one of many factors governing what it is used to mean."
    - **Location/Context**: Section 3.2, Pragmatics.
    - **Significance**: This quote marks a pivotal turn in the argument, shifting from the focus on literal meaning in semantics to the broader consideration of language use in pragmatics, highlighting the complexity of language communication.

   - **What it is**: Reductio ad absurdum is an argumentation strategy where a proposition is disproved by showing that it leads to an absurd or contradictory conclusion.

4. **Modus Tollens**:
   - **What it is**: Modus tollens is a form of deductive reasoning where if a conditional statement is true and its consequent is false, then its antecedent must also be false.
   - **Where it appears**: The text uses modus tollens in the argument that if an expression is not assigned a meaning, it is not a linguistic expression.
   - **How it functions in the argument**: This strategy is used to argue that "the absolute is one" is not a sentence because it lacks semantic assignment, reinforcing the importance of semantic rules in determining linguistic expressions.

## SECTION 7: CONCEPTUAL APPARATUS

### 7.1 Key Terms and Their Definitions

1. **Proposition**: A proposition is defined as the meaning of a true or false sentence, distinct from the sentence itself but structurally similar to it.

2. **Sentence**: A sentence is defined as a linguistic expression that expresses a proposition, composed of discrete parts that correspond to parts of the proposition.

3. **Digital Structure**: A digital structure is defined as a structure composed of discrete, isolable entities, such as sentences and propositions.

4. **Analog Structure**: An analog structure is defined as a structure that does not decompose into minimal significant units, such as visual perceptions.

5. **Semantics**: Semantics is defined as the branch of the philosophy of language that studies the literal meaning of utterances.

6. **Pragmatics**: Pragmatics is defined as the branch of the philosophy of language that studies the use of language, including both literal and non-literal meanings.

7. **Syntax**: Syntax is defined as the branch of the philosophy of language that studies the structures of the meanings of complex expressions.

### 7.2 Technical Vocabulary Introduced or Employed

1. **Proposition-Isomorph**: The term "proposition-isomorph" is introduced to describe the structural similarity between sentences and propositions.

2. **Morpheme**: The term "morpheme" is employed to refer to the minimal units of significance in a sentence, such as "Sally" and "Bob."

3. **Complex Expression**: The term "complex expression" is used to refer to an expression that consists of other expressions, such as "the man who ate my cookie."

### 7.3 Distinctions Drawn Between Related Concepts

1. **Sentences vs. Propositions**: The text draws a distinction between sentences and propositions, arguing that sentences express propositions but are not identical to them.

2. **Literal vs. Non-Literal Meaning**: The text distinguishes between literal and non-literal meanings, arguing that semantics studies the former while pragmatics studies the latter.

3. **Digital vs. Analog Structures**: The text distinguishes between digital and analog structures, arguing that sentences and propositions are digital while visual perceptions are analog.

### 7.4 Theoretical Innovations or Neologisms

1. **Proposition-Isomorph**: The term "proposition-isomorph" is a theoretical innovation introduced to describe the structural similarity between sentences and propositions.

2. **Sentence-Imposter**: The term "sentence-imposter" is introduced to describe expressions that appear to be sentences but lack semantic assignment, such as "the absolute is one."

### 7.5 Repurposing of Existing Concepts

1. **Digital and Analog**: The text repurposes the concepts of digital and analog from computer science and technology to describe the structures of sentences, propositions, and visual perceptions.

2. **Morpheme**: The text repurposes the linguistic concept of morpheme to describe the minimal units of significance in sentences and propositions.

1. **Introduction of Key Concepts**: The text begins by introducing the concept of sentences as proposition-isomorphs and the nature of propositions as digital structures.

3. **Clear Distinction Between Literal and Non-Literal Meaning**: The text's assumption of a clear distinction between literal and non-literal meanings could be challenged by views that see meaning as more fluid and context-dependent.

### 9.4 Alternative Interpretations or Objections Not Addressed

1. **Holistic Views of Meaning**: The text does not address holistic views of meaning, which hold that the meaning of a sentence depends on the entire language system, potentially challenging its atomistic view of meaning.

2. **Pragmatic Theories of Meaning**: The text does not engage with pragmatic theories of meaning, which emphasize the role of context and use in determining meaning, potentially challenging its focus on literal meaning in semantics.

3. **Cognitive Linguistic Perspectives**: The text does not consider cognitive linguistic perspectives, which emphasize the role of cognitive processes in shaping language and meaning, potentially challenging its focus on logical analysis.

### 9.5 Scope and Limitations of the Claims

1. **Scope**: The text's claims are primarily focused on the nature of sentences and propositions and the three branches of the philosophy of language, providing a comprehensive framework for understanding language.

2. **Limitations**: The text's focus on the digital nature of propositions and sentences may limit its ability to account for the complexity and variability of language use, particularly in non-literal contexts.

3. **Generalizability**: The text's arguments may be generalizable to other areas of philosophy and linguistics, such as the philosophy of mind and cognitive science, but may require further development to account for the specific contexts and challenges of these fields.

## SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

### 2.1 Core Thesis Statement

The core thesis of the text can be articulated in multiple formulations due to its complexity:

1. **Primary Formulation**: The meaning of complex expressions in language is determined by the semantic structures of their constituent parts, and understanding these structures requires the discipline of semantics.

2. **Secondary Formulation**: The literal meaning of a sentence can be distinguished from what a speaker intends to convey, and semantics is essential for clarifying the structure of literal meaning, especially in cases where this distinction is not immediately clear.

### 2.2 Primary Arguments Supporting the Thesis

1. **Argument 1: Semantic Structure of Complex Expressions**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that the meanings of complex expressions are derived from the meanings of their simpler components. For example, the sentence "Sally hates Bob" is analyzed in terms of the meanings of "Sally," "hates," and "Bob." This argument is supported by the assertion that the relationship between the meaning of "Sally hates Bob" and "hates" is identical to that between "Larry loves Julie" and "loves."
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text provides examples of sentences and their constituent parts to illustrate how the meanings of complex expressions are built from simpler ones. It states, "The discipline of syntax studies the relation that the meanings of complex expressions bear to the meanings of the simple expressions composing them."

2. **Argument 2: Need for Semantics to Clarify Metaknowledge**
   - **Explanation**: The text posits that while speakers may understand the meaning of a sentence like "John wants to catch a 20-pound striped bass," they do not necessarily know what it is that they know. Semantics is needed to provide this metaknowledge, which involves understanding the structure of the meaning.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text uses the example of "John wants to catch a 20-pound striped bass" to show that the sentence does not refer to a specific fish but to a proposition about a property. It states, "Semanticists supply us with the missing metaknowledge," and provides a detailed analysis of the sentence's meaning.

3. **Argument 3: Distinction Between Literal and Non-Literal Meaning**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that semantics is necessary to distinguish between what is literally meant by an utterance and what the speaker intends to convey. This distinction is crucial in cases where the literal meaning is not immediately clear.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text provides examples of utterances where the literal meaning is clear (e.g., "the cops are coming") and others where it is not (e.g., "that man is a thief"). It states, "First of all, we must distinguish what is literally meant by an utterance from what it is that the speaker wishes to convey."

### 2.3 Secondary Arguments and Subsidiary Claims

1. **Subsidiary Claim 1: Syntax Studies Semantic Structure**
   - **Explanation**: The text claims that syntax, while not directly studying the meanings of complex expressions, examines the structures of those meanings. This claim supports the primary argument about the importance of understanding semantic structures.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text states, "Syntax doesn't study the semantics (meanings) of complex expressions. It studies the structures of the semantics of complex expressions. Syntax studies semantic structure."

2. **Subsidiary Claim 2: Propositions and Properties in Sentence Meaning**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that sentences like "John wants to catch a 20-pound striped bass" do not refer to specific entities but to propositions and properties. This claim supports the need for semantics to clarify the structure of meaning.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text provides a detailed analysis of the sentence, showing that it refers to a proposition about a property rather than a specific fish. It states, "(1) doesn't describe a relationship between John and some non-existent or quasi-existent fish. It affirms the existence of a relationship between John and a proposition."

### 2.4 Implicit Assumptions Underlying the Arguments

1. **Assumption 1: Compositionality of Meaning**
   - **Explanation**: The text assumes that the meaning of a complex expression is composed of the meanings of its parts. This assumption underlies the argument about the semantic structure of complex expressions.

2. **Assumption 2: Distinction Between Knowledge and Metaknowledge**
   - **Explanation**: The text assumes that understanding a sentence's meaning is different from understanding the structure of that meaning. This assumption supports the argument about the need for semantics to provide metaknowledge.

3. **Assumption 3: Importance of Literal Meaning**
   - **Explanation**: The text assumes that distinguishing between literal and non-literal meaning is crucial for understanding language. This assumption underlies the argument about the role of semantics in clarifying literal meaning.

### 2.5 Logical Structure of the Overall Argument

The text's overall argument follows a logical structure that can be outlined as follows:

1. **Premise 1**: The meanings of complex expressions are derived from the meanings of their simpler components.
2. **Premise 2**: Understanding the structure of these meanings requires the discipline of semantics.
3. **Premise 3**: Semantics is necessary to clarify the metaknowledge involved in understanding sentences.
4. **Premise 4**: There is a distinction between what is literally meant by an utterance and what the speaker intends to convey.
5. **Conclusion**: Therefore, semantics is essential for understanding the structure of meaning in language, particularly in distinguishing literal from non-literal meaning.

### 2.6 Argument Progression and Development Throughout the Text

The text's argument progresses through several stages:

1. **Introduction of Key Concepts**: The text begins by introducing the concepts of syntax and semantics, explaining how syntax studies the structures of meanings rather than the meanings themselves.

2. **Example Analysis**: It then provides examples of sentences ("Sally hates Bob," "Larry loves Julie") to illustrate how the meanings of complex expressions are derived from their parts.

3. **Need for Semantics**: The text then argues for the necessity of semantics to clarify the metaknowledge involved in understanding sentences, using the example of "John wants to catch a 20-pound striped bass."

4. **Distinction Between Literal and Non-Literal Meaning**: The argument progresses to discuss the importance of distinguishing between literal and non-literal meaning, using examples like "the cops are coming" and "that man is a thief."

5. **Conclusion**: The text concludes by reinforcing the central thesis that semantics is essential for understanding the structure of meaning in language, particularly in cases where the distinction between literal and non-literal meaning is not clear.

## SECTION 4: ANALYTICAL MOVES DEPLOYED

### 4.1 Conceptual Analysis and Distinctions Drawn

1. **Conceptual Analysis**
   - **Explanation**: Conceptual analysis involves breaking down complex concepts into their constituent parts to understand their meanings. The text employs this technique to analyze the meanings of sentences like "Sally hates Bob" and "John wants to catch a 20-pound striped bass."
   - **Where it Appears**: Throughout the text, particularly in the analysis of the sentences mentioned above.
   - **How it Functions**: It helps to clarify the meanings of complex expressions by showing how they are composed of simpler components. For example, the text breaks down "Sally hates Bob" into "Sally," "hates," and "Bob" to show how the meaning of the sentence depends on the meanings of these parts.

2. **Distinctions Drawn**
   - **Explanation**: The text draws distinctions between related concepts, such as syntax and semantics, literal and non-literal meaning, and propositions and properties.
   - **Where it Appears**: The distinction between syntax and semantics is drawn early in the text, while the distinction between literal and non-literal meaning is discussed later.
   - **How it Functions**: These distinctions help to clarify the roles of different linguistic disciplines and the different aspects of meaning that need to be considered. For example, the distinction between syntax and semantics helps to explain why syntax studies the structures of meanings rather than the meanings themselves.

### 4.2 Argumentation Strategies

1. **Reductio ad Absurdum**
   - **Explanation**: Reductio ad absurdum is an argumentation strategy that involves showing that a proposition leads to an absurd or contradictory conclusion, thereby disproving the proposition.
   - **Where it Appears**: The text uses this strategy when discussing the sentence "John wants to catch a 20-pound striped bass," arguing that it cannot be about a specific fish because there is no such fish that John must catch to satisfy the desire.
   - **How it Functions**: It helps to refute the idea that the sentence refers to a specific fish, leading to the conclusion that it must refer to a proposition about a property.

2. **Modus Tollens**
   - **Explanation**: Modus tollens is a form of argument that involves denying the consequent of a conditional statement to infer the denial of the antecedent.
   - **Where it Appears**: The text uses this strategy when discussing the sentence "John wants to catch a 20-pound striped bass," stating that if the sentence were about a specific fish, then there would be a fish x such that John must catch x to satisfy the desire. Since there is no such fish, the sentence cannot be about a specific fish.
   - **How it Functions**: It helps to logically infer that the sentence must be about a proposition rather than a specific fish.

### 4.3 Category Error Detection

## SECTION 7: CONCEPTUAL APPARATUS

### 7.1 Key Terms and Their Definitions

1. **Semantics**: The study of meaning in language, particularly the relationships between signs and what they denote.
   - **Definition in Text**: "The discipline of semantics... supplies us with the missing metaknowledge" about the structure of meaning.

2. **Syntax**: The study of the rules governing the structure of sentences in a language.
   - **Definition in Text**: "The discipline of syntax studies the relation that the meanings of complex expressions bear to the meanings of the simple expressions composing them."

3. **Literal Meaning**: The meaning of an utterance as determined by its linguistic form, without considering the speaker's intentions or context.
   - **Definition in Text**: "First of all, we must distinguish what is literally meant by an utterance from what it is that the speaker wishes to convey."

4. **Non-Literal Meaning**: The meaning of an utterance that goes beyond its literal meaning, often involving the speaker's intentions or context.
   - **Definition in Text**: "What the speaker wishes to convey" is distinguished from what is literally meant.

5. **Proposition**: An abstract entity that can be expressed by a sentence and that can be true or false.
   - **Definition in Text**: "(1) doesn't describe a relationship between John and some non-existent or quasi-existent fish. It affirms the existence of a relationship between John and a proposition."

6. **Property**: An abstract entity that can be instantiated by objects and that can be expressed by a predicate.
   - **Definition in Text**: "The property of being a 20-pound striped bass that John catches is instantiated."

### 7.2 Technical Vocabulary Introduced or Employed

1. **Metaknowledge**: Knowledge about the structure of meaning, as opposed to knowledge of the meaning itself.
   - **Use in Text**: "Semanticists supply us with the missing metaknowledge" about the structure of meaning.

2. **Instantiated**: The state of a property being exemplified or realized by an object.
   - **Use in Text**: "The property of being a 20-pound striped bass that John catches is instantiated."

3. **Grammatically Isomorphic**: Having the same grammatical structure.
   - **Use in Text**: "Given that (1) and (2) are grammatically isomorphic, it's natural to assume..."

### 7.3 Distinctions Drawn Between Related Concepts

1. **Syntax vs. Semantics**: Syntax studies the structures of meanings, while semantics studies the meanings themselves.
   - **Explanation in Text**: "Syntax doesn't study the semantics (meanings) of complex expressions. It studies the structures of the semantics of complex expressions."

2. **Literal vs. Non-Literal Meaning**: Literal meaning is determined by the linguistic form of an utterance, while non-literal meaning involves the speaker's intentions or context.
   - **Explanation in Text**: "First of all, we must distinguish what is literally meant by an utterance from what it is that the speaker wishes to convey."

3. **Propositions vs. Properties**: Propositions are abstract entities that can be true or false, while properties are abstract entities that can be instantiated by objects.
   - **Explanation in Text**: "(1) doesn't describe a relationship between John and some non-existent or quasi-existent fish. It affirms the existence of a relationship between John and a proposition." "The property of being a 20-pound striped bass that John catches is instantiated."

### 7.4 Theoretical Innovations or Neologisms

1. **No Theoretical Innovations or Neologisms**: The text does not introduce any new theoretical concepts or neologisms. It relies on established linguistic and philosophical terminology to analyze the structure of meaning.

### 7.5 Repurposing of Existing Concepts

Instead of referring to a specific fish, the sentence is shown to refer to the property of being a 20-pound striped bass that John catches.

## SECTION 1: DOMAIN & DISCIPLINARY CONTEXT

### 1.1 Primary Academic Domain(s) and Subdomain(s)

1. **Linguistics**: The text primarily falls within the domain of linguistics, specifically within the subdomain of semantics. Semantics is concerned with the meaning of words and sentences, and the text delves deeply into the nuances of literal meaning and how it is perceived and understood.

2. **Philosophy of Language**: The text also engages with the philosophy of language, a subdomain of philosophy that explores the nature, origins, and use of language. It addresses questions about meaning, reference, and the relationship between language and reality.

### 1.2 Interdisciplinary Connections and Influences

1. **Cognitive Science**: The discussion of how perceptual information influences the understanding of literal meaning connects to cognitive science, which studies how the mind processes information.

2. **Psychology**: The text touches on psychological aspects of language comprehension, particularly how different descriptions of the same individual can lead to different interpretations of the same utterance.

3. **Computer Science**: The concept of semantic rules and the systematic pairing of sentences with meanings is relevant to natural language processing, a field within computer science.

### 1.3 Historical Positioning within the Field

1. **Early 20th Century**: The text's focus on semantics and the philosophy of language can be traced back to early 20th-century developments, such as the work of Gottlob Frege, who distinguished between sense and reference, and Ludwig Wittgenstein, who explored the relationship between language and reality.

2. **Mid to Late 20th Century**: The text aligns with the later developments in semantics and philosophy of language, particularly the work of philosophers like Donald Davidson and linguists like Noam Chomsky, who have contributed to the understanding of meaning and language structure.

### 1.4 Relationship to Major Schools of Thought

1. **Analytic Philosophy**: The text's focus on precise analysis of language and meaning aligns with the analytic tradition, which emphasizes clarity and logical rigor in philosophical inquiry.

2. **Pragmatics**: While the text primarily deals with semantics, it also touches on pragmatic aspects, such as how context influences meaning, which is a key concern in the pragmatic school of thought.

### 1.5 Target Audience and Scholarly Community

1. **Linguists**: The text is primarily aimed at linguists interested in semantics and the nuances of meaning in language.

2. **Philosophers of Language**: Philosophers who study the nature of language and meaning will find the text relevant to their work.

3. **Cognitive Scientists**: Researchers in cognitive science, particularly those studying language processing, may find the text's insights into perception and meaning useful.

4. **Students and Scholars**: The text is suitable for advanced students and scholars in linguistics, philosophy, and related fields who are interested in the intricacies of semantic analysis.

## SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

### 2.1 Core Thesis Statement

The core thesis of the text is that the literal meaning of utterances can be obscured by the pre-semantic information through which they are grasped, necessitating the intervention of professional semanticists to uncover the true meaning.

### 2.2 Primary Arguments Supporting the Thesis

1. **Argument from Different Descriptions**: The text argues that the same literal meaning can be grasped through different perceptual descriptions, leading to a lack of awareness that the same meaning is being affirmed. For example, the utterances U1 and U2 both mean "x is a thief," but the speaker does not realize this because they are based on different visual descriptions of Steve.

   - **Explanation**: The text illustrates this with the example of Steve, who is described differently in two scenarios (wearing a ski-mask and running off vs. being a portly amicable fellow sitting in a chair). Despite the different descriptions, the literal meaning of both utterances is the same, but the speaker does not recognize this due to the different perceptual information.

2. **Argument from Contextual Relativity**: The text argues that the meaning of degree properties like "smart" is contextually relative, and this relativity can obscure the literal meaning of sentences.

   - **Explanation**: The example of Fido and Timmy being described as "smart" is used to show that the property of being smart is relative to a benchmark (smart for a dog vs. smart for a human). This relativity means that the literal meaning of "smart" in different contexts can be different, requiring semantic analysis to clarify.

3. **Argument from Semantic Rules**: The text argues that languages are sets of semantic rules that systematically pair sentences with meanings, and understanding these rules is crucial for uncovering literal meanings.

   - **Explanation**: The text explains that the English language assigns meanings to sentences and sentences to meanings through semantic rules. This systematic pairing is essential for understanding how literal meanings are determined and can be obscured by pre-semantic information.

### 2.3 Secondary Arguments and Subsidiary Claims

1. **Subsidiary Claim on Ambiguity**: The text addresses the claim that words like "smart" might be ambiguous, but rejects this as a solution to the problem of contextual relativity.

   - **Explanation**: The text argues that treating "smart" as ambiguous (like "dumb") is not a plausible solution because it does not account for the contextual relativity of degree properties.

2. **Subsidiary Claim on Implicit Relationality**: The text suggests that degree properties like "smart" are implicitly relational, meaning they are understood relative to a contextually determined benchmark.

   - **Explanation**: This claim is supported by the example of Fido being smart relative to other dogs, and Timmy being smart relative to other humans. The text argues that this implicit relationality affects the literal meaning of sentences.

### 2.4 Implicit Assumptions Underlying the Arguments

1. **Assumption of Perceptual Influence**: The text assumes that perceptual information significantly influences the understanding of literal meaning, which is why different descriptions can lead to different interpretations of the same utterance.

2. **Assumption of Contextual Importance**: The text assumes that context plays a crucial role in determining the meaning of degree properties, which is why the same word can have different meanings in different contexts.

3. **Assumption of Semantic Complexity**: The text assumes that the literal meanings of sentences are often complex and obscured, requiring specialized knowledge to uncover them.

### 2.5 Logical Structure of the Overall Argument

The text's overall argument follows a deductive structure, moving from specific examples to general conclusions about the nature of literal meaning and the role of semantics:

1. **Premise 1**: Different perceptual descriptions can lead to the same literal meaning being grasped differently (illustrated by the Steve example).
2. **Premise 2**: Degree properties like "smart" are contextually relative and can have different meanings in different contexts (illustrated by the Fido and Timmy example).
3. **Premise 3**: Languages are sets of semantic rules that systematically pair sentences with meanings (general statement about language).
4. **Conclusion**: The literal meaning of utterances can be obscured by pre-semantic information, necessitating the intervention of professional semanticists to uncover the true meaning.

### 2.6 Argument Progression and Development Throughout the Text

1. **Introduction of the Problem**: The text begins by presenting the problem of how the same literal meaning can be affirmed without the speaker realizing it, using the example of Steve and the cupcakes.

2. **Exploration of Perceptual Influence**: The text then explores how different perceptual descriptions can lead to the same literal meaning being grasped differently, using the example of Steve in different scenarios.

3. **Introduction of Contextual Relativity**: The text introduces the concept of contextual relativity with the example of Fido and Timmy being described as "smart," showing how the same word can have different meanings in different contexts.

4. **Rejection of Ambiguity**: The text rejects the solution of treating words like "smart" as ambiguous, arguing that this does not account for the contextual relativity of degree properties.

5. **Proposal of Implicit Relationality**: The text proposes that degree properties are implicitly relational, meaning they are understood relative to a contextually determined benchmark.

6. **Discussion of Semantic Rules**: The text discusses how languages are sets of semantic rules that systematically pair sentences with meanings, emphasizing the importance of understanding these rules for uncovering literal meanings.

7. **Conclusion**: The text concludes by reiterating the need for professional semanticists to uncover the true literal meanings of sentences, given the complexity and obscurity of these meanings.

## SECTION 3: REPRESENTATIVE QUOTATIONS

1. **Quotation**: "that man is a thief."
   - **Location/Context**: This is the utterance U2, made by the speaker after seeing Steve eat a cupcake.
   - **Significance**: This quotation captures the main example used to illustrate how the same literal meaning can be affirmed without the speaker realizing it, due to different perceptual descriptions.

2. **Quotation**: "There is some x such that x just ate my cupcake and such that U2 is true exactly if: x is a thief."
   - **Location/Context**: This is part of the explanation of the literal meaning of U2.
   - **Significance**: This quotation defines the literal meaning of U2 and sets up the argument that the same meaning can be affirmed without the speaker realizing it.

3. **Quotation**: "Unbeknownst to me, Steve is the pick-pocket, and there is some individual x, namely Steve, such that each of U1 and U2 is true if and only if x is a thief."
   - **Location/Context**: This is part of the explanation of how U1 and U2 have the same literal meaning.
   - **Significance**: This quotation highlights the fact that the speaker does not realize that U1 and U2 have the same literal meaning, due to different perceptual descriptions.

4. **Quotation**: "My uttering U1 was a response to my being given a visual description of Steve. That description was to the effect that: (i) there is some man x such that x is wearing a ski-mask and such that x is running off into the distance."
   - **Location/Context**: This is part of the explanation of how U1 was formed.
   - **Significance**: This quotation illustrates how perceptual information influences the understanding of literal meaning, using the example of Steve wearing a ski-mask.

5. **Quotation**: "But U1's literal meaning is not that that effect. There is some man x such that x is a wearing a ski-mask (etc.), such that in uttering U1 I was saying that: (ii) x is a thief."
   - **Location/Context**: This is part of the explanation of the literal meaning of U1.
   - **Significance**: This quotation clarifies that the literal meaning of U1 is "x is a thief," despite the perceptual description of Steve wearing a ski-mask.

6. **Quotation**: "The meaning of U1, being identical with (ii), is quite threadbare. But I grasped that threadbare meaning through my descriptively rich visual perception, whose content is given by (i)."
   - **Location/Context**: This is part of the explanation of how the literal meaning of U1 is grasped.
   - **Significance**: This quotation emphasizes the distinction between the literal meaning of U1 and the perceptual information through which it is grasped.

7. **Quotation**: "My uttering U2 was a response to my being given a different description of Steve. That description was to the effect that: (iii) there is some man x such that x is a portly amicable fellow who is sitting over in that chair."
   - **Location/Context**: This is part of the explanation of how U2 was formed.
   - **Significance**: This quotation illustrates how a different perceptual description of Steve leads to the same literal meaning being affirmed in U2.

8. **Quotation**: "But U2's literal meaning is not that that effect. There is some man x such that x is a portly amicable fellow (etc.) such that in uttering U2 I was saying that: (ii) x is a thief."
   - **Location/Context**: This is part of the explanation of the literal meaning of U2.
   - **Significance**: This quotation clarifies that the literal meaning of U2 is "x is a thief," despite the perceptual description of Steve being a portly amicable fellow.

9. **Quotation**: "Because I grasped (ii) by way of different bodies of perceptual (descriptive) information, I didn't know, when uttering U2, that what I was affirming was the same thing I was affirming in uttering U1."
   - **Location/Context**: This is part of the explanation of why the speaker did not realize that U1 and U2 have the same literal meaning.
   - **Significance**: This quotation highlights the central point of the argument that different perceptual information can lead to the same literal meaning being affirmed without the speaker realizing it.

10. **Quotation**: "Oftentimes, literal meaning is cloaked by the pre-semantic information through which it is grasped, and semanticists are needed to uncloak it."
    - **Location/Context**: This is the concluding statement of the section on U1 and U2.
    - **Significance**: This quotation encapsulates the main thesis of the text, emphasizing the need for professional semanticists to uncover the true literal meanings of sentences.

## SECTION 4: ANALYTICAL MOVES DEPLOYED

### 4.1 Conceptual Analysis and Distinctions Drawn

1. **Literal Meaning vs. Perceptual Description**: The text draws a distinction between the literal meaning of an utterance and the perceptual description through which it is grasped. This distinction is crucial for understanding how the same literal meaning can be affirmed without the speaker realizing it.

   - **Explanation**: The text uses the examples of U1 and U2 to illustrate this distinction. U1 and U2 both have the literal meaning "x is a thief," but they are based on different perceptual descriptions of Steve (wearing a ski-mask vs. being a portly amicable fellow).

2. **Degree Properties and Contextual Relativity**: The text analyzes the concept of degree properties like "smart" and argues that their meanings are contextually relative. This analysis is used to explain why the same word can have different meanings in different contexts.

   - **Explanation**: The text uses the example of Fido and Timmy being described as "smart" to illustrate how the property of being smart is relative to a benchmark (smart for a dog vs. smart for a human). This relativity means that the literal meaning of "smart" in different contexts can be different.

### 4.2 Argumentation Strategies

1. **Reductio ad Absurdum**: The text uses a reductio ad absurdum argument to reject the solution of treating words like "smart" as ambiguous.

   - **Explanation**: The text argues that if "smart" were ambiguous like "dumb," then "Timmy is smart" and "Fido is smart" would have different meanings. However, this leads to the absurd conclusion that "Timmy is smart" must be true if "Fido is smart" is true, which is not the case. Therefore, the text concludes that "smart" is not ambiguous.

2. **Modus Tollens**: The text uses a modus tollens argument to support the claim that degree properties are implicitly relational.

   - **Explanation**: The text argues that if degree properties were not implicitly relational, then the same word would have the same meaning in different contexts. However, this is not the case (as shown by the Fido and Timmy example). Therefore, the text concludes that degree properties must be implicitly relational.

### 4.3 Category Error Detection

1. **Category Error in Ambiguity**: The text detects a category error in the solution of treating words like "smart" as ambiguous.

   - **Explanation**: The text argues that treating "smart" as ambiguous is a category error because it does not account for the contextual relativity of degree properties. Ambiguity implies different meanings within the same context, whereas contextual relativity implies different meanings across different contexts.

### 4.4 Counterfactual Reasoning

1. **Counterfactual Scenario**: The text uses counterfactual reasoning to illustrate how different perceptual descriptions can lead to the same literal meaning being affirmed without the speaker realizing it.

## SECTION 8: ARGUMENTATIVE STRUCTURE

### 8.1 Overall Organization of the Argument

The text's argument is organized into several sections, each building on the previous one to support the main thesis:

1. **Introduction of the Problem**: The text begins by presenting the problem of how the same literal meaning can be affirmed without the speaker realizing it, using the example of Steve and the cupcakes.

2. **Exploration of Perceptual Influence**: The text then explores how different perceptual descriptions can lead to the same literal meaning being grasped differently, using the example of Steve in different scenarios.

## SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

### 2.1 Core Thesis Statement

The core thesis of the text is that semantic rules assign meanings to properties of physical objects rather than to expressions, and that these rules are not mathematical functions in the strict logical sense.

### 2.2 Primary Arguments Supporting the Thesis

1. **Argument 1: Meaning Requires Animate Beings**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that something becomes an expression only when it is endowed with meaning by animate beings. This is supported by the example of a twig deposit forming the shape of "1 + 1 = 2," which would not be an expression in a world without animate beings.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text states, "something isn't a sentence until a meaning has been assigned to it. In a world where there were no animate beings, but in which the forthcoming parenthetical ink deposit (1 + 1 = 2) was formed out of twigs, that twig deposit wouldn't be an expression of any kind."

2. **Argument 2: Semantic Rules Assign Meanings to Non-Expressions**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that semantic rules assign meanings to non-expressions, specifically to properties of physical objects. This is illustrated by the example of creating a code name for Larry, where the semantic rule assigns the meaning "Larry" to the property of certain bursts of noise.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text states, "Thus, semantic rules assign meanings to non-expressions. But which non-expressions? A story will give us the answer."

3. **Argument 3: Semantic Rules Are Not Mathematical Functions**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that semantic rules are not mathematical functions in the strict logical sense. This is supported by the critique of the function-theoretic view, which posits that semantic rules are mathematical functions that assign meanings to expressions.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text states, "The just-described view is false. The rule denoted by '+' has always existed and always will. Of course, the expression '+' hasn't always existed. But that's irrelevant, since things pre-exist the expressions we use to denote them."

### 2.3 Secondary Arguments and Subsidiary Claims

1. **Subsidiary Claim 1: Expressions Already Have Meanings**
   - **Explanation**: The text claims that expressions already have meanings and do not need to be assigned them. This is supported by the argument that something becomes an expression only when it is endowed with meaning.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text states, "Thus, expressions aren't assigned meanings. They already have them and don't need to be assigned them."

2. **Subsidiary Claim 2: Semantic Rules Assign Meanings to Properties**
   - **Explanation**: The text claims that semantic rules assign meanings to properties of physical objects, specifically morphological or acoustical properties. This is supported by the example of assigning the meaning "Larry" to the property of certain bursts of noise.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text states, "Thus semantic rules assign meanings to properties of physical objects-to morphological or acoustical properties (in other words, to properties that a things has in virtue of having a certain shape or sounding a certain way)."

3. **Subsidiary Claim 3: Language is a Set of Semantic Rules**
   - **Explanation**: The text claims that language is a set of semantic rules that assign meanings to properties of physical objects. This is supported by the argument that semantic rules are not mathematical functions in the strict logical sense.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text states, "A semantic rule is therefore something which assigns a meaning to a property, and language is a set of such rules."

### 2.4 Implicit Assumptions Underlying the Arguments

1. **Assumption 1: Meaning is Dependent on Animate Beings**
   - **Explanation**: The text assumes that meaning is dependent on the intentions and understanding of animate beings. This is implicit in the argument that something becomes an expression only when it is endowed with meaning by animate beings.

2. **Assumption 2: Properties of Physical Objects Can Be Assigned Meanings**
   - **Explanation**: The text assumes that properties of physical objects, such as morphological or acoustical properties, can be assigned meanings. This is implicit in the argument that semantic rules assign meanings to properties of physical objects.

3. **Assumption 3: Semantic Rules Are Not Mathematical Functions**
   - **Explanation**: The text assumes that semantic rules are not mathematical functions in the strict logical sense. This is implicit in the critique of the function-theoretic view of semantic rules.

### 2.5 Logical Structure of the Overall Argument

The overall argument of the text follows a deductive structure, with the core thesis being supported by a series of primary and secondary arguments. The text begins by establishing the foundational claim that something becomes an expression only when it is endowed with meaning by animate beings. From this, it deduces that semantic rules assign meanings to non-expressions, specifically to properties of physical objects. The text then critiques the function-theoretic view of semantic rules, arguing that they are not mathematical functions in the strict logical sense. This leads to the conclusion that language is a set of semantic rules that assign meanings to properties of physical objects.

### 2.6 Argument Progression and Development Throughout the Text

1. **Introduction of Core Concepts**: The text begins by introducing the core concepts of expressions and semantic rules, arguing that something becomes an expression only when it is endowed with meaning by animate beings.

2. **Development of Primary Arguments**: The text then develops its primary arguments, first arguing that semantic rules assign meanings to non-expressions, specifically to properties of physical objects. This is illustrated by the example of creating a code name for Larry.

3. **Critique of Alternative Views**: The text then critiques the function-theoretic view of semantic rules, arguing that they are not mathematical functions in the strict logical sense. This is supported by the example of the rule denoted by "+" and the semantic rule for "Socrates."

4. **Conclusion and Implications**: The text concludes by stating that language is a set of semantic rules that assign meanings to properties of physical objects. It also notes that this is only an approximation to the truth, with further qualifications found in Section 7.5.

## SECTION 4: ANALYTICAL MOVES DEPLOYED
