SECTION 1: DOMAIN & DISCIPLINARY CONTEXT

1. Primary academic domain(s) and subdomain(s):
   1.1. The primary academic domain of the text is Philosophy, specifically within the subdomain of Philosophy of Language.
   1.2. It also touches upon Linguistics, particularly in the area of Semantics.

2. Interdisciplinary connections and influences:
   2.1. The text draws on insights from Psychology, particularly in the discussion of the Gricean approach, which emphasizes the psychological aspects of meaning.
   2.2. There are also connections to Cognitive Science, as the text considers how meaning is processed and understood by individuals.
   2.3. The discussion of semantic rules and their normative nature connects to Ethics, as it touches on the concept of correctness in language use.

3. Historical positioning within the field:
   3.1. The text situates itself within the ongoing debate about the nature of meaning, which has been a central concern in Philosophy of Language since the early 20th century.
   3.2. It engages with the work of key figures such as H.P. Grice and Ludwig Wittgenstein, positioning itself in the latter half of the 20th century and into the 21st century.
   3.3. The critique of Grice's theory reflects a shift in the field towards more nuanced understandings of meaning that go beyond speaker's intentions.

4. Relationship to major schools of thought:
   4.1. The text aligns with the analytic tradition in philosophy, particularly in its rigorous analysis of concepts and arguments.
   4.2. It engages critically with the Gricean school of thought, which emphasizes the role of speaker's meaning in determining literal meaning.
   4.3. The text also shows influences from the later Wittgenstein's philosophy of language, particularly in its rejection of the idea that meaning is use.

5. Target audience and scholarly community:
   5.1. The primary audience is scholars and students in Philosophy of Language and Linguistics.
   5.2. It is also relevant to those in Cognitive Science and Psychology who are interested in the nature of meaning and communication.
   5.3. The text assumes a high level of familiarity with the field, making it suitable for advanced undergraduate or graduate students and professional academics.

═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

1. Core thesis statement:
   1.1. The core thesis of the text is that semantic rules are not mathematical functions and cannot be understood solely in terms of speaker's meaning, as proposed by Grice and his followers.
   1.2. Instead, semantic rules are normative and compositional, determined by the meanings of their parts and their syntactic arrangement.

2. Primary arguments supporting the thesis:
   2.1. Semantic rules are not mathematical functions:
      2.1.1. Semantic rules have not always existed, unlike mathematical functions which are timeless.
      2.1.2. If semantic rules were mathematical functions, there would exist languages where terms like "Socrates" refer to Lincoln and "snow is white" means all horses weigh 18,000 lbs, which is not the case.
   2.2. Grice's theory of meaning as speaker's meaning is inadequate:
      2.2.1. Literal meaning cannot be reduced to what speakers mean when they utter an expression.
      2.2.2. The meaning of subsentential expressions like "or" cannot be captured by speaker's meaning alone.
      2.2.3. The meaning of sentences is compositional, determined by the meanings of their parts and their syntactic arrangement, not by speaker's intentions.
      2.2.4. Grice's theory fails to account for the normative nature of semantic rules, which dictate what is correct or incorrect in language use.

3. Secondary arguments and subsidiary claims:
   3.1. Wittgenstein's view that "meaning is use" is also inadequate, as it fails to capture the distinction between literal meaning and speaker's meaning.
   3.2. Neo-Gricean attempts to salvage Grice's theory by positing indirect determination of meaning by speaker's intentions are also flawed.

4. Implicit assumptions underlying the arguments:
   4.1. There is a clear distinction between literal meaning and speaker's meaning.
   4.2. Semantic rules are normative, prescribing what is correct or incorrect in language use.
   4.3. The meaning of a sentence is determined by the meanings of its parts and their syntactic arrangement.

5. Logical structure of the overall argument:
   5.1. The text begins by establishing that semantic rules are not mathematical functions, using a reductio ad absurdum argument.
   5.2. It then critiques Grice's theory of meaning as speaker's meaning, using counterexamples and logical analysis to show its inadequacies.
   5.3. The text further refutes neo-Gricean attempts to modify Grice's theory, demonstrating their failure to account for the compositional and normative nature of semantic rules.

6. Argument progression and development throughout the text:
   6.1. The text starts with a broad critique of semantic rules as mathematical functions, setting the stage for a more focused critique of Grice's theory.
   6.2. It then systematically addresses different aspects of Grice's theory, from its inability to account for subsentential meaning to its failure to recognize the compositional nature of meaning.
   6.3. The text concludes by emphasizing the normative nature of semantic rules, which is incompatible with Grice's view of meaning as speaker's intention.

   3.1. The text identifies a category error in Grice's theory, arguing that it conflates the psychological act of meaning something with the semantic property of having a meaning.

## SECTION 1: DOMAIN & DISCIPLINARY CONTEXT

### 1.1 Primary Academic Domain(s) and Subdomain(s)

The text primarily falls within the domain of philosophy, specifically within the subdomain of philosophy of language. It engages deeply with semantic theory, which is a central concern in the philosophy of language, focusing on the nature and function of meaning in language.

### 1.2 Interdisciplinary Connections and Influences

While the text is firmly rooted in philosophy, it also touches on linguistics, particularly in its discussion of semantic rules and their relation to speaker behavior. Additionally, there are connections to cognitive science, as the text considers the psychological reality of semantic rules and their role in guiding speech acts.

### 1.3 Historical Positioning Within the Field

The text positions itself within ongoing debates in the philosophy of language that have been prominent since the early 20th century, particularly those influenced by the works of Ludwig Wittgenstein and the subsequent development of semantic theories. It engages with contemporary discussions around semantic rules, conceptual role semantics, and the nature of meaning.

### 1.4 Relationship to Major Schools of Thought

The text aligns itself with a critical stance towards certain schools of thought within the philosophy of language, particularly those advocating for semantic rules as mere descriptions of speaker behavior (e.g., Nathan Salmon and Scott Soames) and conceptual role semantics (e.g., Hartry Field and Robert Brandom). It positions itself in opposition to these views, suggesting a more normative and psychologically active role for semantic rules.

### 1.5 Target Audience and Scholarly Community

The target audience for this text is primarily scholars and students within the philosophy of language and related fields such as linguistics and cognitive science. It is written at a level that assumes familiarity with key concepts and debates within these areas, making it suitable for advanced undergraduate or graduate students and professional academics.

## SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

### 2.1 Core Thesis Statement

The core thesis of the text is that semantic rules are not merely idealized descriptions of speaker behavior but are normative and psychologically active, guiding speech acts. Additionally, the text argues against conceptual role semantics, asserting that meaning cannot be constituted by use alone.

### 2.2 Primary Arguments Supporting the Thesis

1. **Semantic Rules as Normative and Psychologically Active**
   - The text argues that semantic rules guide speech acts, as evidenced by the example of choosing the sentence "Smith is now living in France" to convey a specific belief. This choice is guided by knowledge of semantic rules, suggesting that these rules are not merely descriptive but normative and psychologically real.

2. **Critique of Semantic Rules as Descriptions of Speaker Behavior**
   - The text criticizes the view held by Nathan Salmon and Scott Soames that semantic rules are idealized descriptions of speaker behavior. It argues that if semantic rules were merely descriptive, they would be psychologically inert and unable to guide speech acts, which contradicts the normative nature of semantic rules.

3. **Incoherence of Conceptual Role Semantics (CRS)**
   - The text argues that CRS, which posits that meaning is determined by use, is incoherent. It contends that expressions must have meaning before they can be used, and thus, use cannot determine meaning. This argument is supported by the distinction between causal determination and identity of meaning and use.

4. **Critique of CRS's View on Inference and Meaning**
   - The text criticizes CRS's claim that a sentence's meaning is determined by what people infer from it. It argues that changes in beliefs affect inferences but not the meanings of sentences, using the example of learning that Aristotle wrote plays to illustrate this point.

### 2.3 Secondary Arguments and Subsidiary Claims

- **Normative Character of Semantic Rules**
  - The text asserts that semantic rules have a normative character, meaning that speaker behavior is answerable to these rules. This is a subsidiary claim supporting the primary argument that semantic rules are not merely descriptive.

- **Distinction Between Meaning and Use**
  - The text draws a clear distinction between meaning and use, arguing that while use can assign new meanings to expressions, it cannot constitute the meaning of an expression. This supports the critique of CRS.

### 2.4 Implicit Assumptions Underlying the Arguments

- **Psychological Reality of Semantic Rules**
  - The text assumes that semantic rules have a psychological reality, meaning that speakers are aware of and guided by these rules when they speak.

- **Normativity of Language**
  - There is an implicit assumption that language is normative, meaning that there are correct and incorrect ways to use language, which are governed by semantic rules.

- **Priority of Meaning Over Use**
  - The text assumes that meaning precedes use, which is crucial for its critique of CRS and its argument that use cannot determine meaning.

### 2.5 Logical Structure of the Overall Argument

The text's overall argument is structured as follows:

1. **Introduction of the Problematic View**
   - The text begins by presenting the view that semantic rules are idealized descriptions of speaker behavior, which it will critique.

2. **Critique of the Problematic View**
   - It then critiques this view, arguing that semantic rules must be normative and psychologically active to guide speech acts.

3. **Introduction of Conceptual Role Semantics**
   - The text introduces CRS as another problematic view, which it will also critique.

4. **Critique of Conceptual Role Semantics**
   - It then critiques CRS, arguing that meaning cannot be constituted by use and that inferences do not determine meaning.

5. **Conclusion**
   - The text concludes by reinforcing its thesis that semantic rules are normative and psychologically active, and that meaning cannot be reduced to use.

### 2.6 Argument Progression and Development Throughout the Text

The argument progresses from a critique of the view that semantic rules are merely descriptive to a critique of CRS. It begins with a specific example of how semantic rules guide speech acts, then broadens its critique to address the general nature of semantic rules and their relationship to speaker behavior. The text then shifts focus to CRS, using examples and logical arguments to demonstrate its incoherence. Throughout, the text builds a case for its thesis by systematically dismantling opposing views and providing supporting arguments for its own position.

## SECTION 3: REPRESENTATIVE QUOTATIONS

1. **The Author's Main Position**
   - **Quote**: "The most natural assumption is that they are idealized descriptions of what people mean when they speak and write (etc.). If this assumption is right, then, given the points just made, the view in question is wrong."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears early in the text, setting up the critique of the view that semantic rules are merely descriptive.
   - **Significance**: It encapsulates the author's main position that semantic rules cannot be merely descriptive, as this would render them psychologically inert and unable to guide speech acts.

2. **Key Supporting Argument**
   - **Quote**: "I know that Smith is now living in France. Wanting to tell you this, I say: 'Smith is now living in France.' Why do I choose this particular sentence? Because, first of all, I know the relevant semantic rules (viz. that 'Smith' refers to Smith, that 'living' refers to a certain property) and, secondly, because I believe that, given these facts about semantics, the sentence in question is the right one to express my belief."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the section critiquing the view that semantic rules are merely descriptive.
   - **Significance**: It provides a concrete example to support the argument that semantic rules are normative and psychologically active, guiding the choice of sentences in speech acts.

3. **Crucial Definition or Conceptual Distinction**
   - **Quote**: "Expressions have meanings. A meaningless burst of noise isn't an expression. If I cough or guffaw, the burst of noise I've produced doesn't have the sort of meaning had by bona fide expressions."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the section discussing the incoherence of Wittgenstein's claim that "meaning is use."
   - **Significance**: It draws a crucial distinction between expressions with linguistic meaning and meaningless noises, which is essential for the critique of conceptual role semantics.

4. **Position Being Criticized or Refuted**
   - **Quote**: "This view is held by Nathan Salmon (2007) and also by Scott Soames (2002). This view is inconsistent with some obvious facts."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the section critiquing the view that semantic rules are merely descriptive.
   - **Significance**: It identifies specific proponents of the view being criticized, adding weight to the critique by naming influential figures in the field.

5. **Methodological Statement**
   - **Quote**: "If semantic rules are just idealizations of speaker-behavior, then speaker-behavior must pre-exist the semantic rules embodied in it."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the section critiquing the view that semantic rules are merely descriptive.
   - **Significance**: It outlines a methodological approach to understanding semantic rules, suggesting that if they are merely descriptive, they cannot guide behavior.

6. **Pivotal Turn in the Argument**
   - **Quote**: "Another problem with the Salmon-Soames view is that it's inconsistent with the normative character of semantic rules."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the section critiquing the view that semantic rules are merely descriptive.
   - **Significance**: It marks a pivotal turn in the argument, shifting from the psychological reality of semantic rules to their normative character.

7. **Critique of Conceptual Role Semantics**
   - **Quote**: "According to a doctrine known as 'conceptual role semantics' (CRS), whose exponents include Hartry Field (1977) and Robert Brandom (1994), for an expression to have a given meaning is simply for it to be used in a certain way. But this isn't correct as we just saw."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the section critiquing conceptual role semantics.
   - **Significance**: It introduces CRS and immediately refutes it, setting the stage for a detailed critique of this view.

8. **Distinction Between Causal Determination and Identity**
   - **Quote**: "There is all the difference in the world between saying: (1) Expression E's having meaning M is causally determined by E's being used in such and such a manner, and (2) Expression E's having meaning M is identical with E's being used in such and such a manner."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the section critiquing conceptual role semantics.
   - **Significance**: It draws a crucial distinction between causal determination and identity, which is central to the critique of CRS.

9. **Critique of CRS's View on Inference and Meaning**
   - **Quote**: "If we learn tomorrow that, contrary to what we previously thought, Aristotle wrote several plays, we'll infer 'somebody who wrote several plays wrote the Nichomachean Ethics' from 'Aristotle wrote the Nichomachean Ethics.' But it doesn't follow that 'Aristotle wrote the Nichomachean Ethics' would have undergone some change in its semantic meaning."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the section critiquing conceptual role semantics.
   - **Significance**: It provides a concrete example to refute CRS's claim that meaning is determined by inferences, showing that changes in beliefs affect inferences but not meanings.

10. **Implication of CRS's View**
    - **Quote**: "CRS entails that every inference is an analytic inference. Once it's learned that Aristotle wrote plays, it becomes, according to CRS, constitutive of the meaning of 'Aristotle wrote the Nichomachean Ethics' that one can infer from it that a playwright wrote the Nichomachean Ethics."
    - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the section critiquing conceptual role semantics.
    - **Significance**: It highlights an absurd implication of CRS, that every inference would be analytic, which supports the argument that CRS is incoherent.

## SECTION 4: ANALYTICAL MOVES DEPLOYED

### 4.1 Conceptual Analysis and Distinctions Drawn

- **Conceptual Analysis**
  - The text engages in conceptual analysis by examining the nature of semantic rules and their relationship to speaker behavior. It analyzes the concept of meaning and its relationship to use, particularly in the critique of Wittgenstein's claim that "meaning is use."
  - **Example**: The text analyzes the concept of semantic rules, arguing that they are not merely descriptive but normative and psychologically active.

- **Distinctions Drawn**
  - The text draws several important distinctions, such as between meaning and use, causal determination and identity, and expressions with linguistic meaning and meaningless noises.
  - **Example**: The distinction between causal determination and identity is crucial for the critique of conceptual role semantics, as it argues that use can causally determine meaning but cannot be identical with it.

### 4.2 Argumentation Strategies

- **Reductio ad Absurdum**
  - The text uses reductio ad absurdum to critique conceptual role semantics, showing that if CRS were true, it would lead to absurd consequences such as the non-existence of sentences.
  - **Example**: The text argues that if CRS were correct, it would entail that every inference is analytic, leading to the absurd consequence that no sentence could mean anything.

- **Modus Tollens**
  - The text employs modus tollens in its critique of the view that semantic rules are merely descriptive, arguing that if semantic rules were merely descriptive, they would be psychologically inert, which is false; therefore, semantic rules are not merely descriptive.
  - **Example**: "If semantic rules are just idealizations of speaker-behavior, then speaker-behavior must pre-exist the semantic rules embodied in it. But if that's the case, then the activity described by semantic rules isn't guided by them. An awareness of those rules is no part of what leads people to say the things they do. Those rules are psychologically inert. They have no 'psychological reality.'"

### 4.3 Category Error Detection

- **Category Error Detection**
  - The text detects a category error in the view that semantic rules are merely descriptive, arguing that this view conflates the descriptive with the normative and psychologically active nature of semantic rules.
  - **Example**: The text argues that semantic rules cannot be merely descriptive because they guide speech acts, which is a normative and psychologically active function.

### 4.4 Counterfactual Reasoning

- **Counterfactual Reasoning**
  - The text uses counterfactual reasoning to critique conceptual role semantics, imagining scenarios where beliefs change to show that inferences do not determine meaning.
  - **Example**: "If we learn tomorrow that, contrary to what we previously thought, Aristotle wrote several plays, we'll infer 'somebody who wrote several plays wrote the Nichomachean Ethics' from 'Aristotle wrote the Nichomachean Ethics.' But it doesn't follow that 'Aristotle wrote the Nichomachean Ethics' would have undergone some change in its semantic meaning."

### 4.5 Thought Experiments

- **Thought Experiments**
  - The text employs thought experiments to illustrate its points, such as the example of choosing the sentence "Smith is now living in France" to convey a belief.
  - **Example**: "I know that Smith is now living in France. Wanting to tell you this, I say: 'Smith is now living in France.' Why do I choose this particular sentence? Because, first of all, I know the relevant semantic rules (viz. that 'Smith' refers to Smith, that 'living' refers to a certain property) and, secondly, because I believe that, given these facts about semantics, the sentence in question is the right one to express my belief."

### 4.6 Analogies and Comparisons

- **Analogies and Comparisons**
  - The text uses analogies and comparisons to clarify its points, such as comparing the meaning of expressions to the meaning of noises like coughs or guffaws.
  - **Example**: "If I cough or guffaw, the burst of noise I've produced doesn't have the sort of meaning had by bona fide expressions. It has, at most, meaning in irrelevant, purely evidential sense, e.g., the sense in which a cough may be evidence of a cold."

### 4.7 Appeals to Intuition

- **Appeals to Intuition**
  - The text appeals to intuition to support its arguments, such as the intuitive understanding that semantic rules guide speech acts.
  - **Example**: The text's example of choosing the sentence "Smith is now living in France" appeals to the reader's intuition that this choice is guided by knowledge of semantic rules.

### 4.8 Inference to Best Explanation

  - The text uses inference to the best explanation to argue that the normative and psychologically active nature of semantic rules best explains how speakers choose their words.
  - **Example**: The text argues that the best explanation for why speakers choose certain sentences is that they are guided by semantic rules, which are normative and psychologically active.

### 4.9 Transcendental Arguments

- **Transcendental Arguments**
  - The text employs transcendental arguments to argue that semantic rules must be normative and psychologically active because without them, speech acts could not be guided.
  - **Example**: The text argues that if semantic rules were not normative and psychologically active, they could not guide speech acts, which is a necessary condition for language use.

### 4.10 Dialectical Moves

## SECTION 7: CONCEPTUAL APPARATUS

### 7.1 Key Terms and Their Definitions

- **Semantic Rules**
  - Defined as normative and psychologically active entities that guide speaker behavior, rather than mere descriptions of speaker behavior.

- **Meaning**
  - Defined as a property of expressions that precedes and is not constituted by their use.

- **Use**
  - Defined as the way expressions are employed in speech acts, which can causally determine but not constitute meaning.

- **Conceptual Role Semantics (CRS)**
  - Defined as the doctrine that the meaning of an expression is determined by its use, which the text critiques as incoherent.

### 7.2 Technical Vocabulary Introduced or Employed

  - Used to describe the character of semantic rules, indicating that they guide and constrain speaker behavior.

  - Used to describe the active role of semantic rules in guiding speaker behavior.

  - Used in the critique of conceptual role semantics to describe the absurd consequence that every inference would be analytic if CRS were true.

  - The text distinguishes between expressions with linguistic meaning and meaningless noises, such as coughs or guffaws.

## SECTION 1: DOMAIN & DISCIPLINARY CONTEXT

### 1.1 Primary Academic Domain(s) and Subdomain(s)

1. **Philosophy of Language**: The text primarily falls within the domain of philosophy of language, specifically focusing on the subdomains of semantics and meaning.
   - **Semantics**: The text delves into the nature of meaning, particularly literal meaning, and how it is determined by the components of language.
   - **Meaning**: The discussion revolves around the concepts of literal and understood meaning, exploring how these concepts relate to simple and complex expressions.

2. **Linguistics**: The text also intersects with linguistics, particularly in the areas of syntax and semantics.
   - **Syntax**: The arrangement of simple expressions into complex ones is discussed, which is a key aspect of syntactic analysis.
   - **Semantics**: The text's focus on the meaning of expressions aligns with semantic studies in linguistics.

### 1.2 Interdisciplinary Connections and Influences

1. **Cognitive Science**: The text's exploration of how people interpret and understand language touches on cognitive processes, which is a key area of study in cognitive science.
   - The discussion of how people put together the meanings of simple expressions to understand complex ones can be seen as an inquiry into cognitive mechanisms of language processing.

2. **Psychology**: The text's consideration of systematic error and misinterpretation in language use connects to psychological theories of perception and cognition.
   - The idea that systematic, widespread error is impossible with simple expressions suggests a psychological constraint on language comprehension.

### 1.3 Historical Positioning Within the Field

1. **Early 20th Century**: The text's focus on the nature of meaning and the distinction between literal and understood meaning can be traced back to early 20th-century philosophers like Frege and Russell, who laid the groundwork for modern semantics.
   - **Frege**: His distinction between sense and reference is echoed in the text's discussion of how expressions refer to objects.
   - **Russell**: His work on definite descriptions and the theory of descriptions is relevant to the text's exploration of how simple expressions contribute to the meaning of complex ones.

2. **Mid to Late 20th Century**: The text also engages with later developments in the philosophy of language, such as the work of Quine, Davidson, and Kripke.
   - **Quine**: His critique of the analytic-synthetic distinction and his views on indeterminacy of translation are relevant to the text's discussion of systematic error and misinterpretation.
   - **Davidson**: His work on radical interpretation and the principle of charity aligns with the text's exploration of how people interpret complex expressions based on their understanding of simple ones.
   - **Kripke**: His theory of naming and necessity, particularly his distinction between rigid and non-rigid designators, is relevant to the text's discussion of how simple expressions like proper names refer to objects.

### 1.4 Relationship to Major Schools of Thought

1. **Analytic Philosophy**: The text aligns with the analytic tradition in philosophy, which emphasizes clarity and precision in language and logic.
   - The text's focus on the logical structure of language and the precise definition of terms like 'literal meaning' and 'understood meaning' is characteristic of analytic philosophy.

2. **Pragmatism**: The text's consideration of how people actually use and interpret language in practice has elements of pragmatism.
   - The discussion of how people's beliefs about the meanings of simple expressions determine the meanings of complex expressions reflects a pragmatic approach to meaning.

### 1.5 Target Audience and Scholarly Community

1. **Philosophers of Language**: The primary audience for this text would be philosophers specializing in the philosophy of language, particularly those interested in semantics and the nature of meaning.
   - The text's detailed analysis of literal and understood meaning, as well as its engagement with historical figures in the field, would be of particular interest to this group.

2. **Linguists**: Linguists with an interest in semantics and syntax would also find the text relevant, especially those studying the relationship between the structure of language and its meaning.
   - The text's discussion of how the arrangement of simple expressions affects the meaning of complex ones would be of interest to linguists.

3. **Cognitive Scientists and Psychologists**: Researchers in cognitive science and psychology who study language processing and comprehension would find the text's exploration of systematic error and misinterpretation relevant.
   - The text's insights into how people interpret language could inform cognitive models of language processing.

## SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

### 2.1 Core Thesis Statement

1. **Primary Formulation**: The literal meaning of a sentence is determined by the meanings of its simple parts and the way those parts are arranged.
   - This thesis is articulated throughout the text, particularly in sections discussing the relationship between simple and complex expressions.

2. **Secondary Formulation**: The literal meaning of simple expressions coincides with what people take them to mean, while the literal meaning of complex expressions may diverge from what people understand them to mean.
   - This formulation is evident in the text's discussion of how systematic error is impossible with simple expressions but possible with complex ones.

### 2.2 Primary Arguments Supporting the Thesis

1. **Argument 1: Literal Meaning of Simple Expressions**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that the literal meaning of simple expressions is determined by what people take them to mean. This is because systematic, widespread error is impossible with simple expressions.
   - **Evidence**: The text uses the example of the word "red" to illustrate this point, stating that if people thought "red" meant what is meant by "blue," then "red" would have that meaning.

2. **Argument 2: Literal Meaning of Complex Expressions**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that the literal meaning of complex expressions is a function of the meanings of their simple parts and the order in which those parts are arranged.
   - **Evidence**: The text uses the example of the sentence "Socrates was more wise than Plato, but he was less sharp then Aristotle" to illustrate how the literal meaning of a complex expression is determined by its simple parts and their arrangement.

3. **Argument 3: Systematic Misinterpretation**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that systematic misinterpretations of sentences arise from people not knowing how to put the meanings of simple expressions together, not from failing to know what the simple parts mean.
   - **Evidence**: The text discusses how people may misunderstand the sentence about Socrates, Plato, and Aristotle, but this misunderstanding is due to difficulty in combining the meanings of the simple parts, not in understanding the simple parts themselves.

### 2.3 Secondary Arguments and Subsidiary Claims

1. **Subsidiary Claim 1: CRS is False**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that the Content-Role Semantics (CRS) theory, which posits that what a sentence means is determined by what we infer from it, is false.
   - **Evidence**: The text states that it is the other way around: what we infer from a sentence is determined by what the sentence already means.

2. **Subsidiary Claim 2: Tokens vs. Types**
   - **Explanation**: The text introduces the distinction between expression-tokens and expression-types, arguing that no word is identical with any utterance of it.
   - **Evidence**: The text provides the example of the word "snow" and its tokens to illustrate this distinction.

### 2.4 Implicit Assumptions Underlying the Arguments

1. **Assumption 1: Semantic Simplicity**
   - The text assumes that there is a clear distinction between simple and complex expressions, and that simple expressions have a straightforward, non-divergent meaning.

2. **Assumption 2: Cognitive Consistency**
   - The text assumes that people's understanding of simple expressions is consistent and reliable, and that any misinterpretation of complex expressions is due to cognitive limitations in combining meanings.

3. **Assumption 3: Linguistic Competence**
   - The text assumes that speakers of a language have a basic level of linguistic competence that allows them to understand the meanings of simple expressions correctly.

### 2.5 Logical Structure of the Overall Argument

1. **Premise 1**: The literal meaning of simple expressions is determined by what people take them to mean.
2. **Premise 2**: The literal meaning of complex expressions is a function of the meanings of their simple parts and the order in which those parts are arranged.
3. **Premise 3**: Systematic misinterpretations of sentences arise from people not knowing how to put the meanings of simple expressions together.
4. **Conclusion**: The literal meaning of a sentence is determined by the meanings of its simple parts and the way those parts are arranged.

### 2.6 Argument Progression and Development Throughout the Text

1. **Introduction of CRS and Its Rejection**: The text begins by introducing and rejecting the Content-Role Semantics theory, setting the stage for its own argument about literal meaning.
2. **Definition of Literal Meaning**: The text then defines literal meaning, distinguishing between simple and complex expressions and explaining how their meanings are determined.
3. **Illustration with Examples**: The text uses examples like "red," "Socrates," and the sentence about Socrates, Plato, and Aristotle to illustrate its points about simple and complex expressions.
4. **Introduction of Tokens vs. Types**: The text introduces the distinction between expression-tokens and expression-types, which is relevant to its discussion of meaning but not directly part of the main argument.
5. **Conclusion and Implications**: The text concludes by summarizing its main points and discussing the implications of its thesis for understanding the nature of meaning.

## SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

### 2.1 Core Thesis Statement

The core thesis of the text is that the semantics of certain linguistic expressions, particularly indexicals and definite descriptions, are determined by context-sensitive rules that assign referents based on the circumstances of utterance. This can be formulated in multiple ways:

1. **Primary Formulation**: The meaning of indexicals and definite descriptions is determined by rules that systematically assign referents based on the context of utterance.

2. **Secondary Formulation**: Indexicals and definite descriptions have a two-dimensional semantic structure, where the meaning of a token is its referent, and the meaning of the type is the rule that assigns that referent.

3. **Tertiary Formulation**: Indexical-free languages are expressively impoverished because they cannot convey perspectival information that relies on context.

### 2.2 Primary Arguments Supporting the Thesis

1. **Argument 1: The Semantics of Indexicals**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that indexicals like "I," "that guy," and "tomorrow" refer to different entities based on the context of utterance. The rule for "I" is that it refers to the person who produced the utterance, while "that guy" refers to a unique, salient individual in the context. This demonstrates that the meaning of indexicals is context-dependent.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text provides examples such as "I am tired" and "that guy is tall" to illustrate how the same expression can refer to different entities in different contexts.

2. **Argument 2: The Two-Dimensional Semantic Structure of Indexicals**
   - **Explanation**: The text posits that indexicals have a two-dimensional semantic structure, where the meaning of a token is its referent, and the meaning of the type is the rule that assigns that referent. This structure allows for the same rule to apply across different contexts, resulting in different referents.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The example of "today" is used to show how the same rule can assign different referents (April 27, 2009, and April 28, 2009) based on the day of utterance.

3. **Argument 3: The Semantics of Definite Descriptions**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that definite descriptions, like indexicals, have a two-dimensional semantic structure. The rule for "the current U.S. President" assigns different referents (Barack Obama and Bill Clinton) based on the time of utterance, but the rule itself remains constant.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text provides examples of how the same description can refer to different individuals at different times, illustrating the context-sensitivity of definite descriptions.

4. **Argument 4: The Expressive Limitations of Indexical-Free Languages**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that languages without indexicals are expressively impoverished because they cannot convey perspectival information that relies on context. This limitation is due to the inability to express information relative to the speaker's perspective.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text uses the example of expressing the weather in Richmond on a specific date to show how an indexical-free language would require cumbersome and less intuitive expressions.

### 2.3 Secondary Arguments and Subsidiary Claims

1. **Demonstratives vs. Indexicals**
   - **Explanation**: The text distinguishes between demonstratives and other indexicals, noting that demonstratives require an accompanying demonstration to eliminate ambiguity in reference.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The example of "that guy" is used to illustrate how pointing can disambiguate the referent when multiple salient individuals are present.

2. **Incomplete Definite Descriptions**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that some definite descriptions are incomplete and require context to pick out a single referent. The context supplies the necessary information to enrich the mediating concept.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The example of "the bald guy" is used to show how context can determine which bald individual is being referred to.

### 2.4 Implicit Assumptions Underlying the Arguments

1. **Contextualism**: The text assumes that the meaning of linguistic expressions is heavily influenced by the context in which they are used. This assumption underlies the entire discussion of indexicals and definite descriptions.

2. **Semantic Rules**: The text assumes that the meaning of expressions can be captured by formal semantic rules that systematically assign referents based on context. This assumption is crucial for the two-dimensional semantic structure argument.

3. **Perspectival Information**: The text assumes that much of the information we convey is perspectival and relies on the speaker's context. This assumption supports the argument about the expressive limitations of indexical-free languages.

### 2.5 Logical Structure of the Overall Argument

The text's overall argument follows a deductive structure, where the core thesis is supported by a series of sub-arguments and examples. The logical flow can be outlined as follows:

1. **Premise 1**: Indexicals refer to different entities based on the context of utterance.
2. **Premise 2**: Indexicals have a two-dimensional semantic structure, where the meaning of a token is its referent, and the meaning of the type is the rule that assigns that referent.
3. **Premise 3**: Definite descriptions, like indexicals, have a two-dimensional semantic structure and are context-sensitive.
4. **Premise 4**: Indexical-free languages are expressively impoverished because they cannot convey perspectival information.
5. **Conclusion**: The semantics of indexicals and definite descriptions are determined by context-sensitive rules that assign referents based on the circumstances of utterance.

### 2.6 Argument Progression and Development Throughout the Text

The text progresses through a series of sections, each building on the previous to develop the overall argument:

1. **Section 7.1**: Introduces the concept of indexicals and their context-sensitivity, laying the groundwork for the core thesis.
2. **Section 7.1.1**: Distinguishes between demonstratives and other indexicals, providing a more nuanced understanding of context-sensitivity.
3. **Section 7.1.2**: Develops the two-dimensional semantic structure argument for indexicals, using examples to illustrate the concept.
4. **Section 7.2**: Extends the two-dimensional semantic structure argument to definite descriptions, showing how they are also context-sensitive.
5. **Section 7.2.1**: Discusses incomplete definite descriptions and how context enriches the mediating concept, further supporting the context-sensitivity argument.
6. **Section 7.3**: Concludes with the argument about the expressive limitations of indexical-free languages, tying together the importance of context in language use.

## SECTION 7: CONCEPTUAL APPARATUS

### 7.1 Key Terms and Their Definitions

1. **Indexicals**: Expressions whose reference depends on the context of utterance. Examples include "I," "that guy," and "tomorrow."

2. **Demonstratives**: A subset of indexicals that require an accompanying demonstration to eliminate ambiguity in reference. Examples include "that guy" when accompanied by pointing.

3. **Mediating Concept**: The concept through which an expression secures reference. For example, the mediating concept for "I" is the person who produced the utterance.

4. **Two-Dimensional Semantic Structure**: A semantic structure where the meaning of a token is its referent, and the meaning of the type is the rule that assigns that referent.

5. **Definite Descriptions**: Expressions that describe a unique individual or object. Examples include "the current U.S. President" and "the whole number that comes right after one."

6. **Incomplete Definite Descriptions**: Definite descriptions that fail to pick out a single object without additional context. An example is "the bald guy."

7. **Indexical-Free Languages**: Languages that do not contain indexicals and are thus expressively impoverished because they cannot convey perspectival information.

### 7.2 Technical Vocabulary Introduced or Employed

1. **Token**: A specific instance of an expression used in a particular context.

2. **Type**: The general form of an expression, independent of any specific context of use.

3. **Context-Sensitive Expressions**: Expressions whose meaning depends on the context in which they are used.

4. **Complex Indexicals**: Indexicals that consist of more than one word, such as "that tall man."

### 7.3 Distinctions Drawn Between Related Concepts

1. **Indexicals vs. Demonstratives**: Indexicals are context-sensitive expressions, while demonstratives are a subset of indexicals that require an accompanying demonstration.

2. **Token vs. Type Meaning**: The meaning of a token is its referent, while the meaning of a type is the rule that assigns that referent.

3. **Complete vs. Incomplete Definite Descriptions**: Complete definite descriptions pick out a single object, while incomplete definite descriptions require additional context to do so.

### 7.4 Theoretical Innovations or Neologisms

1. **Two-Dimensional Semantic Structure**: The text introduces the concept of a two-dimensional semantic structure for indexicals and definite descriptions, where the meaning of a token is its referent, and the meaning of the type is the rule that assigns that referent.

2. **Mediating Concept**: The text introduces the term "mediating concept" to describe the concept through which an expression secures reference.

### 7.5 Repurposing of Existing Concepts

1. **Context-Sensitivity**: The text repurposes the concept of context-sensitivity to apply not only to indexicals but also to definite descriptions, arguing that both types of expressions have a two-dimensional semantic structure.

2. **Semantic Rules**: The text repurposes the concept of semantic rules to describe the rules that assign referents to indexicals and definite descriptions based on context.

## SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

### 2.1 Core Thesis Statement

The core thesis of the text can be articulated in multiple formulations:

1. **Primary Formulation**: The meaning of a sentence token is a proposition, while the meaning of a sentence type is a rule that assigns propositions to its tokens based on the context of utterance.

2. **Secondary Formulation**: Context-sensitive expressions are essential for expressing perspectival thoughts and beliefs, and an indexical-free language would be severely impoverished.

### 2.2 Primary Arguments Supporting the Thesis

1. **Distinction Between Beliefs and Thoughts**: The text argues that different beliefs and thoughts can be held independently of each other, even when they are about the same state of affairs. For example, believing it's 60° in Richmond on Feb. 07, 2009, is different from thinking it's now 60° in Richmond, even if one is in Richmond at the time. This supports the thesis by illustrating the necessity of context-sensitive expressions to capture these nuances.

   - **Explanation**: The text uses the example of temperature beliefs to show that context-sensitive expressions like "now" and "here" are necessary to express specific thoughts and beliefs accurately. Without these expressions, the language would fail to capture the perspectival nature of these beliefs.

2. **Necessity of Indexicals for Perspectival Thoughts**: The text asserts that nothing perspectival can be expressed without using indexicals. This is because indexicals allow speakers to express thoughts about their immediate context, such as "I am thirsty" or "it's now 60° here."

   - **Explanation**: The argument is that indexicals are crucial for expressing thoughts that are tied to the speaker's perspective. Without them, language would be limited to third-person descriptions, which cannot capture the first-person perspective.

3. **Semantic Role of Sentence Types and Tokens**: The text argues that sentence types containing context-sensitive components do not bear truth values; instead, it is the tokens of these types that make statements and bear truth values based on the context of utterance.

   - **Explanation**: This argument supports the thesis by explaining how the meaning of a sentence token is a proposition determined by the context, while the meaning of a sentence type is a rule that assigns these propositions. This distinction is crucial for understanding how context affects meaning.

4. **Context-Sensitivity and Proposition Assignment**: The text posits that the semantic rule for context-sensitive expressions assigns propositions to tokens based on the context of utterance. For example, the truth of "I am tired" depends on who is speaking and when.

   - **Explanation**: This argument illustrates how context-sensitive expressions require a semantic rule that takes into account the context to assign the correct proposition to a token. This supports the thesis by showing the dynamic nature of meaning in context-sensitive language.

### 2.3 Secondary Arguments and Subsidiary Claims

1. **Rationality of Independent Beliefs**: The text claims that one can rationally hold any given belief about a state of affairs without holding others, even if they are about the same state of affairs. This supports the necessity of context-sensitive expressions to capture these rational distinctions.

2. **Impoverishment of Indexical-Free Language**: The text argues that an indexical-free language would be extremely impoverished because it could not express perspectival thoughts like "I am JM" or "I am that guy in the mirror."

3. **Non-Ambiguity of Context-Sensitive Sentences**: The text asserts that context-sensitive sentences are not ambiguous; they have one meaning, which is a rule that assigns propositions to their tokens based on context.

### 2.4 Implicit Assumptions Underlying the Arguments

1. **Contextual Dependence of Meaning**: The text assumes that the meaning of many expressions depends on the context in which they are used, which is a foundational assumption for the arguments about indexicals and context-sensitivity.

2. **Distinction Between Types and Tokens**: The text assumes that there is a meaningful distinction between sentence types and tokens, which is crucial for the argument about how meaning is assigned.

3. **Importance of Perspectival Thoughts**: The text assumes that perspectival thoughts are significant and that language should be capable of expressing them, which underlies the argument about the necessity of indexicals.

### 2.5 Logical Structure of the Overall Argument

The text's overall argument can be broken down into the following logical structure:

1. **Premise 1**: Different beliefs and thoughts about the same state of affairs can be held independently.
2. **Premise 2**: Context-sensitive expressions are necessary to express these independent beliefs and thoughts accurately.
3. **Premise 3**: Sentence types containing context-sensitive components do not bear truth values; instead, tokens of these types make statements and bear truth values based on the context of utterance.
4. **Premise 4**: The semantic rule for context-sensitive expressions assigns propositions to tokens based on the context of utterance.
5. **Conclusion**: The meaning of a sentence token is a proposition, while the meaning of a sentence type is a rule that assigns propositions to its tokens based on the context of utterance.

### 2.6 Argument Progression and Development Throughout the Text

1. **Introduction of Beliefs and Thoughts**: The text begins by distinguishing between different beliefs and thoughts about the same state of affairs, setting the stage for the necessity of context-sensitive expressions.

2. **Necessity of Indexicals**: The argument then progresses to the necessity of indexicals for expressing perspectival thoughts, illustrating the impoverishment of an indexical-free language.

## SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

### 2.1 Core Thesis Statement

The core thesis of the text can be articulated in multiple formulations:

1. **Primary Formulation**: Semantic rules assign meanings to properties, and these meanings are themselves semantic rules that assign meanings to instances of those properties.
2. **Secondary Formulation**: All expressions, including those that appear context-insensitive, have a two-dimensional semantic structure where type-meaning diverges from token-meaning due to context-sensitivity.

### 2.2 Primary Arguments Supporting the Thesis

1. **Semantic Rules and Properties**:
   - The text argues that semantic rules assign meanings to properties, and these meanings are themselves semantic rules. For example, the property of having a morphology similar to the ink deposit "Barack Obama" is assigned a meaning that is a semantic rule, which in turn assigns Barack Obama to any instance of that property.
   - This argument is supported by the example of the ink deposit "Barack Obama," where the semantic rule assigns the meaning of the person Barack Obama to any instance of the property.

2. **Context-Sensitivity of All Expressions**:
   - The text asserts that all expressions, even those that appear context-insensitive, are context-sensitive due to the presence of tense-markers and other context-sensitive components.
   - This is supported by the example of the sentence "the U.S. economy is fairly stable," where the truth of the proposition depends on when the sentence is uttered.

3. **Distinction Between Type-Meaning and Token-Meaning**:
   - The text argues that the meaning of an expression-type (type-meaning) is distinct from the meaning of its tokens (token-meaning) due to context-sensitivity.
   - This is illustrated by the example of the sentence "the interior angles of a Euclidean triangle add up to 180°," where the tense-marker on "add" is context-sensitive, even though the sentence appears to express an atemporal proposition.

4. **Eternal Sentences and Context-Insensitivity**:
   - The text challenges the notion of eternal sentences, which are supposed to express the same proposition regardless of context, by arguing that even these sentences are context-sensitive at the level of literal meaning.
   - This is supported by the example of the sentence "the interior angles of a Euclidean triangle add up to 180°," where the tense-marker is argued to be context-sensitive, even if it is often treated as inert.

### 2.3 Secondary Arguments and Subsidiary Claims

1. **Pragmatics-Based Context-Insensitivity**:
   - The text claims that any apparent context-insensitivity of expressions is a result of pragmatics rather than semantics.
   - This is supported by the example of the sentence "the interior angles of a Euclidean triangle add up to 180°," where the context-insensitivity is attributed to our beliefs about mathematics rather than the sentence's literal meaning.

2. **Proper Names and Two-Dimensional Semantics**:
   - The text argues that even proper names, which might seem to have fixed referents, have a two-dimensional semantic structure.
   - This is illustrated by the example of "Bill Clinton," where the expression-token is a physical entity capable of transmitting information, while the expression-type is an abstract entity.

### 2.4 Implicit Assumptions Underlying the Arguments

1. **Semantic Rules as Fundamental**: The text assumes that semantic rules are the fundamental units of meaning assignment in language.
2. **Context-Sensitivity as Universal**: There is an implicit assumption that all expressions in natural languages are context-sensitive to some degree.
3. **Distinction Between Type and Token**: The text assumes a clear distinction between expression-types and expression-tokens, with the former being abstract and the latter being physical.
4. **Pragmatics and Semantics**: The text assumes that pragmatics can influence our perception of semantic context-sensitivity, even if the literal meaning remains context-sensitive.

### 2.5 Logical Structure of the Overall Argument

The text's overall argument follows a deductive structure, moving from general principles about semantic rules and context-sensitivity to specific examples and conclusions:

1. **Premise 1**: Semantic rules assign meanings to properties, and these meanings are themselves semantic rules.
2. **Premise 2**: All expressions in natural languages contain context-sensitive components, such as tense-markers.
3. **Premise 3**: The meaning of an expression-type (type-meaning) is distinct from the meaning of its tokens (token-meaning) due to context-sensitivity.
4. **Conclusion**: All expressions, including those that appear context-insensitive, have a two-dimensional semantic structure where type-meaning diverges from token-meaning.

### 2.6 Argument Progression and Development Throughout the Text

1. **Introduction of Semantic Rules**: The text begins by introducing the concept of semantic rules and their role in assigning meanings to properties and instances.
2. **Context-Sensitivity of Expressions**: It then moves to discuss the context-sensitivity of all expressions, using examples like "the U.S. economy is fairly stable" to illustrate how context affects meaning.
3. **Analysis of Apparent Context-Insensitivity**: The text then addresses apparent counterexamples, such as "the interior angles of a Euclidean triangle add up to 180°," arguing that even these sentences are context-sensitive at the level of literal meaning.
4. **Eternal Sentences and Pragmatics**: It challenges the notion of eternal sentences and argues that any apparent context-insensitivity is a result of pragmatics rather than semantics.
5. **Proper Names and Two-Dimensional Semantics**: Finally, the text extends its analysis to proper names, arguing that even these have a two-dimensional semantic structure.

## SECTION 8: ARGUMENTATIVE STRUCTURE

### 8.1 Overall Organization of the Argument

The text's argument is organized into several key sections, each building on the previous to support the overall thesis:

1. **Introduction of Semantic Rules**: The text begins by introducing the concept of semantic rules and their role in assigning meanings to properties and instances.
2. **Context-Sensitivity of Expressions**: It then moves to discuss the context-sensitivity of all expressions, using examples to illustrate how context affects meaning.
3. **Analysis of Apparent Context-Insensitivity**: The text addresses apparent counterexamples, arguing that even these sentences are context-sensitive at the level of literal meaning.
4. **Eternal Sentences and Pragmatics**: It challenges the notion of eternal sentences and argues that any apparent context-insensitivity is a result of pragmatics rather than semantics.
5. **Proper Names and Two-Dimensional Semantics**: Finally, the text extends its analysis to proper names, arguing that even these have a two-dimensional semantic structure.

### 8.2 Logical Dependencies Between Claims

The text's argument relies on several logical dependencies between its claims:

1. **Semantic Rules and Properties**: The claim that semantic rules assign meanings to properties depends on the definition of semantic rules as hierarchical structures.
2. **Context-Sensitivity of All Expressions**: The claim that all expressions are context-sensitive depends on the presence of tense-markers and other context-sensitive components in all sentences.
3. **Distinction Between Type-Meaning and Token-Meaning**: The claim that type-meaning diverges from token-meaning depends on the context-sensitivity of expressions.
4. **Eternal Sentences and Context-Insensitivity**: The claim that eternal sentences are context-sensitive at the level of literal meaning depends on the argument that all expressions are context-sensitive.
5. **Proper Names and Two-Dimensional Semantics**: The claim that proper names have a two-dimensional semantic structure depends on the distinction between expression-types and expression-tokens.

### 8.3 Progression from Premises to Conclusions

The text progresses from premises to conclusions in a deductive manner:

1. **Premise 1**: Semantic rules assign meanings to properties, and these meanings are themselves semantic rules.
2. **Premise 2**: All expressions in natural languages contain context-sensitive components, such as tense-markers.
3. **Premise 3**: The meaning of an expression-type (type-meaning) is distinct from the meaning of its tokens (token-meaning) due to context-sensitivity.
4. **Conclusion**: All expressions, including those that appear context-insensitive, have a two-dimensional semantic structure where type-meaning diverges from token-meaning.

The text uses several examples to illustrate its points:

1. **"Barack Obama"**: The text uses the example of the ink deposit "Barack Obama" to illustrate how a semantic rule assigns a meaning to a property.
2. **"The U.S. economy is fairly stable"**: The text uses this example to illustrate how the truth of a proposition depends on the context in which it is uttered.
3. **"The interior angles of a Euclidean triangle add up to 180°"**: The text uses this example to challenge the notion of eternal sentences and argue that even this sentence is context-sensitive at the level of literal meaning.
4. **"Bill's various holdings add up to $180,000,000"**: The text uses this example to compare with the sentence about Euclidean triangles and argue that both are context-sensitive.
5. **"That man" and "the current President"**: The text uses these examples to illustrate how the meaning of expressions can vary based on context.

1. **Objection to Context-Sensitivity**: The text anticipates the objection that some expressions are context-insensitive and responds by arguing that all expressions contain context-sensitive components like tense-markers.
2. **Objection to Eternal Sentences**: The text anticipates the objection that some sentences are eternal and responds by challenging the notion of eternal sentences and arguing that even these sentences are context-sensitive at the level of literal meaning.

## SECTION 9: CRITICAL EVALUATION INDICATORS

### 9.1 Strengths of the Argument

1. **Comprehensive Analysis**: The text provides a comprehensive analysis of semantic rules and their role in assigning meanings to properties and instances.
2. **Clear Distinctions**: The text draws clear distinctions between type-meaning and token-meaning, and between context-sensitivity and context-insensitivity.
3. **Empirical Examples**: The text uses empirical examples from natural languages to support its arguments about semantic rules and context-sensitivity.
4. **Engagement with Opposing Views**: The text engages with and challenges opposing views on eternal sentences and context-insensitivity, strengthening its own argument.

### 9.2 Potential Weaknesses or Gaps

1. **Overgeneralization**: The text's argument that all expressions are context-sensitive might be seen as an overgeneralization, as some expressions might be less context-sensitive than others.
2. **Lack of Empirical Data**: While the text uses empirical examples, it lacks a systematic empirical study to support its claims about the universal context-sensitivity of expressions.
3. **Complexity of Semantic Rules**: The text's focus on the hierarchical nature of semantic rules might be seen as overly complex, potentially obscuring simpler explanations of meaning assignment.

### 9.3 Unstated Assumptions that Could be Challenged

1. **Semantic Realism**: The text assumes semantic realism, the view that semantic rules and meanings are objective features of language, which could be challenged by semantic anti-realists.
2. **Universal Context-Sensitivity**: The text assumes that all expressions are context-sensitive, which could be challenged by those who argue that some expressions are genuinely context-insensitive.
3. **Rationalist Approach**: The text assumes a rationalist approach to understanding meaning, focusing on the logical structure of language, which could be challenged by those who emphasize empirical data.

### 9.4 Alternative Interpretations or Objections Not Addressed

1. **Alternative Theories of Meaning**: The text does not address alternative theories of meaning, such as those based on use or social practices, which could provide different explanations of meaning assignment.

## SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

### 2.1 Core Thesis Statement

The core thesis of the text can be formulated in multiple ways due to its complexity:

1. **Primary Formulation**: Expression-types, unlike expression-tokens, are properties and thus non-spatiotemporal entities, which cannot mediate information or function as symbols. Therefore, semantic rules must assign meanings differently to types and tokens, necessitating a two-dimensional approach to semantics.

2. **Secondary Formulation**: The logical form of statements often diverges from their grammatical form, particularly in statements containing quantifiers, which are about properties rather than specific objects.

### 2.2 Primary Arguments Supporting the Thesis

1. **Argument 1: Expression-Types as Properties**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that expression-types are properties, which are non-spatiotemporal entities. This is supported by the claim that properties per se, as opposed to their instances, are assigned meanings, and properties are non-spatiotemporal.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text states, "expression-types, unlike expression-tokens, are properties and are therefore non-spatiotemporal entities."

2. **Argument 2: Non-Symbolic Nature of Types**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that non-spatiotemporal entities cannot mediate information or function as symbols. Since expression-types are non-spatiotemporal, they cannot be symbols.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text states, "it makes no sense to suppose a nonspatiotemporal entity could mediate information or, therefore, could in any significant sense be a symbol."

3. **Argument 3: Semantic Functions of Types and Tokens**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that due to the profound metaphysical differences between tokens and types, it would be theoretically arbitrary to suppose that types could discharge the same semantic functions as their tokens.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text states, "given the profound metaphysical differences between tokens and types, it would be theoretical arbitrariness of the worst kind to suppose that types could discharge the same semantic functions as their tokens."

4. **Argument 4: Two-Dimensional Semantics**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that proper names, indexicals, and definite descriptions have two-dimensional semantics, with semantic rules assigning meanings differently to types and tokens.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text states, "In general, proper names, no less than indexicals and definite descriptions have two-dimensional semantics."

5. **Argument 5: Logical Form and Quantifiers**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that statements containing quantifiers are about properties rather than specific objects, and their logical form diverges from their grammatical form.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text states, "Some statements that, given their grammatical forms, appear to be about objects are in fact about properties."

### 2.3 Secondary Arguments and Subsidiary Claims

1. **Subsidiary Claim 1: Semantic Rules for Types and Tokens**
   - **Explanation**: The text claims that semantic rules assign meanings differently to properties (types) and their instances (tokens). The meaning assigned to a type is a rule, while the meaning assigned to a token is a proposition.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text states, "the rules that assign meanings to properties are semantic rules, since anything that assigns meaning to anything is ipso facto a semantic rule."

2. **Subsidiary Claim 2: Frege's Insight on Logical Form**
   - **Explanation**: The text claims that Frege was the first to recognize the divergence between logical and grammatical forms, particularly in statements about properties.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text states, "Frege was the first to see clearly that logical and grammatical may diverge-he was the first to grasp the very idea of such a divergence."

### 2.4 Implicit Assumptions Underlying the Arguments

1. **Assumption 1: Realism About Properties**: The text assumes a realist stance on properties, treating them as real entities that can be assigned meanings.

2. **Assumption 2: Distinction Between Types and Tokens**: The text assumes a clear and meaningful distinction between expression-types and expression-tokens, which is foundational to its arguments about semantics and metaphysics.

3. **Assumption 3: Semantic Rules as Fundamental**: The text assumes that semantic rules are fundamental to understanding the meanings of linguistic expressions, both at the level of types and tokens.

4. **Assumption 4: Importance of Logical Form**: The text assumes that understanding the logical form of statements is crucial for understanding their true meaning, particularly in cases where logical and grammatical forms diverge.

### 2.5 Logical Structure of the Overall Argument

The overall argument of the text can be broken down into two main parts:

1. **Part 1: Semantics of Types and Tokens**
   - **Premise 1**: Expression-types are properties and are non-spatiotemporal entities.
   - **Premise 2**: Non-spatiotemporal entities cannot mediate information or function as symbols.
   - **Premise 3**: There are profound metaphysical differences between tokens and types.
   - **Conclusion 1**: Types cannot discharge the same semantic functions as tokens, necessitating a two-dimensional approach to semantics.

2. **Part 2: Logical Form and Quantifiers**
   - **Premise 1**: Some statements, given their grammatical forms, appear to be about objects but are in fact about properties.
   - **Premise 2**: Statements containing quantifiers are about properties rather than specific objects.
   - **Premise 3**: The logical form of such statements diverges from their grammatical form.
   - **Conclusion 2**: Understanding the logical form of statements is crucial for understanding their true meaning, particularly in cases involving quantifiers.

### 2.6 Argument Progression and Development Throughout the Text

1. **Introduction of the Type-Token Distinction**: The text begins by introducing the distinction between expression-types and expression-tokens, arguing that types are properties and thus non-spatiotemporal entities.

2. **Non-Symbolic Nature of Types**: The text then argues that types cannot mediate information or function as symbols, leading to the conclusion that types and tokens must have different semantic functions.

3. **Two-Dimensional Semantics**: The text introduces the concept of two-dimensional semantics, arguing that proper names, indexicals, and definite descriptions have two-dimensional semantics, with semantic rules assigning meanings differently to types and tokens.

4. **Semantic Rules for Types and Tokens**: The text then discusses how semantic rules assign meanings to properties (types) and their instances (tokens), with the meaning assigned to a type being a rule and the meaning assigned to a token being a proposition.

## SECTION 7: CONCEPTUAL APPARATUS

### 7.1 Key Terms and Their Definitions

1. **Expression-Type**: An expression-type is defined as a property, which is a non-spatiotemporal entity.

2. **Expression-Token**: An expression-token is defined as an instance of an expression-type.

3. **Semantic Rule**: A semantic rule is defined as anything that assigns meaning to anything, including rules that assign meanings to properties and their instances.

4. **Logical Form**: Logical form is defined as the underlying structure of a statement that represents its true meaning, which may diverge from its grammatical form.

5. **Grammatical Form**: Grammatical form is defined as the surface structure of a statement, which may not accurately represent its true meaning.

6. **Quantifier**: A quantifier is defined as an expression having the property that, if a sentence contains it, that sentence is ipso facto to the effect that the extension of one property has a certain degree of overlap with the extension of some other property.

### 7.2 Technical Vocabulary Introduced or Employed

1. **Two-Dimensional Semantics**: The text introduces the technical vocabulary of two-dimensional semantics, which involves semantic rules assigning meanings differently to types and tokens.

2. **Constant Function**: The text employs the technical term "constant function" to describe the semantic rule corresponding to a proper name.

3. **Proposition**: The text employs the technical term "proposition" to describe the meaning assigned to a token by a semantic rule.

### 7.3 Distinctions Drawn Between Related Concepts

1. **Type-Token Distinction**: The text draws a distinction between expression-types and expression-tokens, arguing that types are properties and tokens are instances of those properties.

2. **Property-Instance Distinction**: The text draws a distinction between properties per se and their instances, arguing that semantic rules assign meanings differently to properties and their instances.

3. **Logical-Grammatical Form Distinction**: The text draws a distinction between the logical and grammatical forms of statements, arguing that they often diverge, particularly in statements containing quantifiers.

### 7.4 Theoretical Innovations or Neologisms

1. **Two-Dimensional Semantics**: The text's advocacy for a two-dimensional approach to semantics can be seen as a theoretical innovation, as it applies this approach to proper names, indexicals, and definite descriptions.

2. **Semantic Rules for Types and Tokens**: The text's argument that semantic rules assign meanings differently to properties (types) and their instances (tokens) can be seen as a theoretical innovation, as it distinguishes between the meanings assigned to types and tokens.

### 7.5 Repurposing of Existing Concepts
