## SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

### 2.1 Core Thesis Statement

1. **Primary Formulation**: Quantified sentences in natural language are pseudo-objectual statements that appear to be about objects but are really about properties, necessitating a reparse to expose their logical forms.
   - This thesis is articulated in the opening sentence: "concerns properties. In natural language, quantified sentences are pseudo-objectual statements. They appear to be about objects, but are really about properties. That is why they must be reparsed if their logical forms are to be exposed."

2. **Secondary Formulation**: The divergence between a statement's grammatical form and its logical form is a consequence of the need to define certain expressions contextually.
   - This is evident in the passage: "The divergence between SS's grammatical and logical forms is obviously a consequence of the fact that 'someone' must be defined contextually."

### 2.2 Primary Arguments Supporting the Thesis

1. **Argument 1: Quantified Sentences and Properties**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that quantified sentences, such as "someone snores," are not about specific individuals but about the instantiation of properties. This is supported by the claim that "someone" does not refer to any specific individual but rather to the instantiation of a property.
   - **Supporting Passage**: "So, for example, 'someone' doesn't refer to anyone. It doesn't refer to John or Sally or Jane. That is why, given any proper name N, the statement (NS) ‹N doesn't snore› is compatible with (SS) 'someone snores.'"

2. **Argument 2: Contextual Definition and Logical Form**
   - **Explanation**: The text asserts that the divergence between grammatical and logical forms is due to the need for contextual definitions. Expressions like "someone" must be defined in terms of how they affect the meaning of sentences containing them, rather than by direct reference to objects.
   - **Supporting Passage**: "The fact that grammatical and logical form sometimes diverge is related to the fact some expressions are to be defined contextually. To define an expression contextually is to how, in virtue of containing it, a sentence's meaning is affected."

3. **Argument 3: Frege's Contribution**
   - **Explanation**: The text credits Frege with discovering the divergence between grammatical and logical forms, which is foundational to modern analytic philosophy. This argument supports the thesis by establishing a historical and philosophical context for the analysis of logical forms.
   - **Supporting Passage**: "Frege discovered both facts and, therewith, created modern analytic philosophy."

### 2.3 Secondary Arguments and Subsidiary Claims

1. **Subsidiary Claim 1: Not All Contextual Definitions Lead to Divergence**
   - **Explanation**: The text acknowledges that not all expressions defined contextually lead to a divergence between grammatical and logical forms. This claim refines the primary argument by specifying the conditions under which such divergences occur.
   - **Supporting Passage**: "Nonetheless, it would be an overstatement to say that whenever a sentence contains an expression that must be defined contextually, it's grammatical form pulls apart from its logical form."

2. **Subsidiary Claim 2: Denotative Definitions and Logical Form**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that expressions that can be defined denotatively, such as proper names like "Socrates," do not necessarily lead to a divergence between grammatical and logical forms. This claim further refines the primary argument by distinguishing between different types of definitions.
   - **Supporting Passage**: "Whenever an expression can be defined denotatively, a sentence's logical form will not, by virtue of that sentence's containing that expression, diverge from its logical form."

### 2.4 Implicit Assumptions Underlying the Arguments

1. **Assumption 1: Logical Form as the True Structure**
   - **Explanation**: The text assumes that the logical form of a sentence is its true or underlying structure, which may differ from its surface grammatical form. This assumption underpins the entire analysis of quantified sentences and contextual definitions.

2. **Assumption 2: Importance of Context in Meaning**
   - **Explanation**: The text assumes that the meaning of expressions is heavily dependent on the context in which they are used. This assumption is crucial for the argument about contextual definitions and their impact on logical form.

3. **Assumption 3: Frege's Authority**
   - **Explanation**: The text assumes that Frege's insights into the divergence between grammatical and logical forms are authoritative and foundational to the field. This assumption supports the historical and philosophical context provided for the thesis.

### 2.5 Logical Structure of the Overall Argument

1. **Premise 1**: Quantified sentences in natural language are pseudo-objectual statements about properties rather than objects.
2. **Premise 2**: The logical form of a sentence may diverge from its grammatical form.
3. **Premise 3**: This divergence is due to the need to define certain expressions contextually.
4. **Premise 4**: Frege discovered the divergence between grammatical and logical forms, which is foundational to modern analytic philosophy.
5. **Conclusion**: Therefore, to understand the true logical structure of quantified sentences, they must be reparsed to expose their logical forms, which are about properties rather than objects.

### 2.6 Argument Progression and Development Throughout the Text

1. **Introduction of the Thesis**: The text begins by introducing the thesis that quantified sentences are pseudo-objectual and must be reparsed to expose their logical forms.
2. **Explanation of Contextual Definition**: The text then explains the concept of contextual definition and how it relates to the divergence between grammatical and logical forms.
3. **Example of "Someone"**: The text provides an example of the expression "someone" to illustrate how it does not refer to specific individuals but to the instantiation of properties.
4. **Clarification of Scope and Limits**: The text clarifies that not all contextual definitions lead to a divergence between grammatical and logical forms, refining the scope of the argument.
5. **Discussion of Denotative Definitions**: The text discusses denotative definitions and how they do not necessarily lead to a divergence between grammatical and logical forms, further refining the argument.
6. **Historical Context with Frege**: The text situates the argument within the historical context of Frege's contributions to logic and philosophy of language, reinforcing the foundational nature of the thesis.
7. **Conclusion**: The text concludes by reiterating the importance of reparing quantified sentences to expose their logical forms, which are about properties rather than objects.

8. **Quote**: "How can it be said that all definitions are contextual definitions? Aren't there also denotative definitions?"

### 4.2 Argumentation Strategies

1. **Reductio ad Absurdum**
   - **Explanation**: The text uses a form of reductio ad absurdum to argue that if "someone" referred to a specific individual, then statements like "N doesn't snore" would be incompatible with "someone snores," which is not the case.
   - **Location/Context**: Section 8.1, discussing the meaning of "someone."
   - **Function in the Argument**: This strategy supports the claim that "someone" does not refer to specific individuals but to the instantiation of properties.

2. **Modus Tollens**
   - **Explanation**: The text employs modus tollens to argue that since the statement "someone snores" is compatible with "N doesn't snore," it follows that "someone" does not refer to any specific individual N.
   - **Location/Context**: Section 8.1, discussing the meaning of "someone."
   - **Function in the Argument**: This strategy supports the thesis that quantified sentences are about properties rather than objects.

## SECTION 7: CONCEPTUAL APPARATUS

### 7.1 Key Terms and Their Definitions

1. **Quantified Sentences**: Sentences that use quantifiers like "someone" or "nobody" to express general statements about properties rather than specific objects.
   - **Evidence**: "In natural language, quantified sentences are pseudo-objectual statements."

2. **Pseudo-Objectual Statements**: Statements that appear to be about objects but are actually about properties.
   - **Evidence**: "They appear to be about objects, but are really about properties."

3. **Logical Form**: The underlying structure of a sentence that reveals its true meaning, which may diverge from its grammatical form.
   - **Evidence**: "It immediately follows that a statement's grammatical form may diverge from its logical form."

4. **Grammatical Form**: The surface structure of a sentence as it appears in natural language.
   - **Evidence**: "It immediately follows that a statement's grammatical form may diverge from its logical form."

5. **Contextual Definition**: A definition of an expression in terms of how it affects the meaning of sentences containing it.
   - **Evidence**: "To define an expression contextually is to how, in virtue of containing it, a sentence's meaning is affected."

6. **Denotative Definition**: A definition of an expression by specifying what it denotes or refers to.
   - **Evidence**: "A denotative definition of an expression E says what E means by saying what it denotes."

### 7.2 Technical Vocabulary Introduced or Employed

1. **Psi**: A placeholder for any property in logical notation.
   - **Evidence**: "‹Someone has psi› is true just in case the property of being a psi is instantiated."

2. **N**: A placeholder for any proper name in logical notation.
   - **Evidence**: "given any proper name N, the statement (NS) ‹N doesn't snore› is compatible with (SS) 'someone snores.'"

3. **E**: A placeholder for any expression in logical notation.
   - **Evidence**: "Let E be an arbitrary expression that can be defined denotatively."

4. **O**: A placeholder for any object in logical notation.
   - **Evidence**: "to say that E refers to O is to make a statement about effect a sentence's containing E has on its truth-conditions."

### 7.3 Distinctions Drawn Between Related Concepts

1. **Grammatical Form vs. Logical Form**: The text distinguishes between the surface structure of a sentence (grammatical form) and its underlying structure (logical form).
   - **Evidence**: "It immediately follows that a statement's grammatical form may diverge from its logical form."

2. **Contextual Definition vs. Denotative Definition**: The text distinguishes between defining an expression in terms of its effect on sentence meaning (contextual definition) and defining it by what it denotes (denotative definition).
   - **Evidence**: "How can it be said that all definitions are contextual definitions? Aren't there also denotative definitions?"

3. **Objectual vs. Property-Based Interpretations**: The text distinguishes between interpreting quantified sentences as being about specific objects (objectual) and as being about the instantiation of properties (property-based).
   - **Evidence**: "They appear to be about objects, but are really about properties."

### 7.4 Theoretical Innovations or Neologisms

1. **Pseudo-Objectual Statements**: The text introduces the term "pseudo-objectual statements" to describe quantified sentences that appear to be about objects but are actually about properties.
   - **Evidence**: "In natural language, quantified sentences are pseudo-objectual statements."

### 7.5 Repurposing of Existing Concepts

1. **Function**: The text repurposes the mathematical concept of a function to explain Frege's generalization and its role in understanding the divergence between grammatical and logical forms.
   - **Evidence**: "As we'll see in Chapter 7, what made it possible for Frege to revolutionize logic was his insight that grammatical and logical form diverge; and what made the latter insight possible was his generalization of the concept of a mathematical function."
